The Principle of Ghayah: Isqat and Ithbat
Definition and application of ghayah in wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Ghayat al-Isqat and Ghayat al-Ithbat and apply them to determine that washing the elbows and ankles in wudu is obligatory.

Core Idea
A limit that is of the same genus as the limited object is included in the ruling; otherwise, it merely marks the endpoint.

The first principle in Kitab al-Taharat of al-Hidayah is a linguistic rule that determines whether a specified limit (ghayah) is itself included in the ruling or merely extends the ruling up to that point. This principle has a direct application in wudu, where the Quran commands washing the hands 'to the elbows' and the feet 'to the ankles'. Does 'to' include the elbows and ankles? The Hanafi answer, based on this principle, is yes—because elbows are of the same genus as hands, and ankles are of the same genus as feet. This lesson unpacks the two types of ghayah and shows how they derive the obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of ghayah
From the passage, note that ghayah is divided into Ghayat al-Isqat (exclusionary limit) and Ghayat al-Ithbat (inclusionary limit). Ghayat al-Isqat is of the same genus as the limited term and is included in the ruling; Ghayat al-Ithbat is of a different genus and is not included.
The key criterion is whether the limit is of the same genus (jins) as the limited object.
2
Apply the principle to the wudu verse
The Quranic verse (Al-Ma'idah 5:6) says 'wash your hands to the elbows' and 'your feet to the ankles'. Determine the genus of 'elbow' and 'ankle'. Elbow is part of the hand, ankle is part of the foot—thus both are of the same genus as the limited terms.
This satisfies the condition for Ghayat al-Isqat.
3
Derive the ruling
Since the ghayah is Ghayat al-Isqat, the limit (elbow/ankle) is included in the ruling. Therefore, washing the elbows and ankles is obligatory (fard) as part of washing the hands and feet.
The opposite would be if the limit were of a different genus, e.g., if the verse said 'to the shoulder', the shoulder would not be washed.
4
State the Hanafi position and its proof
The Hanafi school holds that washing the elbows and ankles is fard. The proof is the Quranic verse interpreted through this usul principle. There is no intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on this point.
Understanding this principle helps in interpreting other ghayah constructions in the Quran and Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارات
(۱) إِنَّ الْغَايَةَ إِنْ كَانَتْ لِإِسْقَاطِ مَا وَرَاءَ تَدْخُلُ تَحْتَ الْمُغَيَّا وَإِنْ كَانَتْ بِمَةِ الْحُكْمِ لَا تَدْخُلُ . .
ترجمہ: غایہ اگر اپنے ماوراء کو ساقط کرنے کے لئے ہو تو مغیا کے تحت داخل ہوگی ، اور اگر یہ صرف حکم کو کھینچنے کے لئے ہو تو پھر غایہ مغیا میں داخل نہیں ہوگی۔
تشریح : اصول مذکورہ اور اس پر ذکر کی گئی تفریح سے قبل اس بات کا جانناضروری ہے کہ غایہ کی دو قسمیں ہیں:
[1] غایت اسقاط [۲] غایت اثبات
غایت اثبات کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ کسی چیز کی غایہ حکم میں شامل نہ ہو لیکن اس حکم کو کھینچ کر اپنے مغیا تک پہنچا دے، اور غایت اسقاط کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ وہ غایت خود تو حکم میں شامل ہو لیکن اپنے علاوہ کو اس حکم میں شامل نہ ہونے دے۔ ان دونوں کا دارو مدار صد ر کلام پر ہے، یعنی غایہ اگر اپنے ماقبل مغیا کی جنس سے ہے تو وہ غایہ غایت اسقاط کہلائے گی ، اور حکم میں شامل ہوگی ، اور اگر غایہ مغیا کی جنس میں سے نہ ہو تو پھر وہ غایہ غایت اثبات کہلائے گی اور حکم میں داخل نہ ہوگی۔ صاحب ہدایہ اصول مذکورہ کو ذکر کر کے اس بات کو ثابت کر رہے ہیں کہ دونوں کہنیوں اور دونوں ٹخنوں کو بھی وضو میں دھونا ضروری ہے، جس طرح ہاتھوں اور پیروں کا دھونا ضروری ہے۔ اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ 'مرفق اور کعب‘ دونوں ید اور رجل‘ کی جنس میں سے ہیں اور یہ غایہ غایت اسقاط ہے اور مغیا میں داخل ہے، لہذا جس طرح ید اور رجل‘ کا دھونا وضو میں ضروری ہے اسی طرح ان کی غایہ یعنی 'مرفق‘ اور 'کعب‘ کا دھونا بھی وضو میں ضروری ہوگا۔ البتہ مذکورہ غایہ چونکہ غایت اسقاط ہے، اس لئے مرفق اور کعب کے علاوہ دیگر حصے مغیا کے حکم سے خارج ہوں گے اور ان کا دھونا ضروری نہیں ہوگا۔
Book of Purification. (1) Indeed, the ghayah (limit/end) if it is for the purpose of dropping what is beyond (isqat), it enters under the mushar (that which is limited). And if it is for the purpose of attracting the ruling (ithbat), it does not enter. Translation: If the ghayah is for dropping what is beyond, it will be included under the limited; and if it is only for attracting the ruling, then the ghayah will not be included in the limited. Commentary: Before discussing the mentioned principle and the furu' (subsidiary issues) mentioned on it, it is necessary to know that the ghayah has two types: [1] Ghayat al-Isqat (limit of exclusion) [2] Ghayat al-Ithbat (limit of inclusion). The meaning of Ghayat al-Ithbat is that the ghayah of something is not included in the ruling but it attracts the ruling up to its limited. And the meaning of Ghayat al-Isqat is that the ghayah itself is included in the ruling but does not allow anything else to be included in that ruling. The basis of both is the context of the speech: if the ghayah is of the same genus as the preceding limited, then that ghayah is called Ghayat al-Isqat and will be included in the ruling; and if the ghayah is not of the genus of the limited, then that ghayah is called Ghayat al-Ithbat and will not enter the ruling. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to prove that both elbows and both ankles must be washed in wudu, just as washing the hands and feet is necessary. The reason is that 'marfiq (elbow) and ka'b (ankle)' are both of the genus of 'yad (hand)' and 'rijl (foot)', and this ghayah is Ghayat al-Isqat and is included in the limited. Therefore, just as washing the hand and foot is necessary in wudu, similarly washing their ghayah, i.e., the elbow and ankle, is also necessary in wudu. However, since the mentioned ghayah is Ghayat al-Isqat, excluding the elbow and ankle, other parts are outside the ruling of the limited and their washing is not necessary.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage introduces the first usul principle from Kitab al-Taharat of Hidayah. The principle concerns the linguistic function of the word 'ghayah' (limit/end), such as 'until' (ila). The author distinguishes two types: Ghayat al-Isqat (exclusionary limit) and Ghayat al-Ithbat (inclusionary limit). The former is when the limit is of the same kind as the limited object, so it is included in the ruling; the latter is when the limit is not of the same kind, so it serves only to extend the ruling up to that point but is not itself included. The text then applies this to the verse of wudu (Al-Ma'idah 5:6), where the limits for washing the hands and feet are 'marafiqa' (elbows) and 'ka'bayn' (ankles). Since elbows are part of the hand and ankles are part of the foot, they are of the same genus, thus they are Ghayat al-Isqat and must be washed. Conversely, the area beyond them (upper arm, shin) is not included. This principle is crucial for correctly determining the extent of obligatory washing in wudu. The passage is taken from the commentary (Sharh) of Hidayah, where the author expands on the base text (matn) by explaining the types and application.

Key Terms
غایہ (ghayah): A limit or endpoint mentioned in a text, often introduced by 'ila' (to/until).
غایت اسقاط (ghayat al-isqat): A limit that is of the same genus as the limited object, so it is included in the ruling and excludes what is beyond.
غایت اثبات (ghayat al-ithbat): A limit that is not of the same genus as the limited object, so it is not included in the ruling but merely marks the endpoint.
مغیا (mushar): That which is limited by the ghayah; the object or action to which the ruling applies up to the limit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle of ghayah, why must the elbows be washed in wudu?
A) Because the word 'ila' always includes the limit
B) Because the elbow is of the same genus as the hand (yad), making it Ghayat al-Isqat
C) Because it is sunnah to wash beyond the required area
D) Because the verse explicitly mentions the elbow as part of the hand

Key Takeaway
The ruling on whether a limit (ghayah) is included or excluded depends on its genus: if same genus, it is included (Ghayat al-Isqat); if different, it is not (Ghayat al-Ithbat).

The Rule of Ghāyah: Inclusion vs. Exclusion of Endpoints
Explains the two types of ghāyah and their effect on legal rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ghāyat isqāṭ and ghāyat ithbāt, and apply the Hanafi rule to determine whether an endpoint is included in a ruling.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, an endpoint of the same genus as the object is included in the ruling; otherwise it is not.

This passage from al-Hidayah introduces a key principle in Hanafi usul al-fiqh regarding grammatical endpoints. It tackles a practical dispute: do the elbows and ankles enter the washing obligation in wudū? The answer hinges on whether the endpoint is one of abrogation (ghāyat isqāṭ) or of extension (ghāyat ithbāt). Understanding this distinction will sharpen your ability to interpret similar Qur'anic and hadith limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Read the first part of the passage which states: if a ghāyah is for isqāṭ (excluding what is beyond), it enters under the mugheyyā; if for madd al-ḥukm (extending the ruling), it does not.
This is the core principle that governs all subsequent analysis.
2
Apply the rule to wudū
The Qur'an commands washing hands 'to the elbows'. Determine whether the elbows are ghāyat isqāṭ or ghāyat ithbāt based on the nature of the hand and elbow.
The elbows are part of the same limb as the hand, so they are of the same genus.
3
Examine Imam Zufar's objection
Imam Zufar argues that endpoints are never included, citing the analogy of fasting where the night (an endpoint) is not part of the fast.
His argument relies on a universal claim that ghāyah always excludes its own point.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The rebuttal distinguishes the genus: elbows are of the same genus as the hand, while the night is of a different genus from fasting. Thus the analogy fails.
The key distinction is whether the endpoint is ghāyat isqāṭ (same genus) or ghāyat ithbāt (different genus).
5
Derive the practical consequence
If a person washes up to but not including the elbows, wudū is invalid according to the majority view. This is the thamarah al-khilaf.
The practical ruling is that the elbows and ankles must be washed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الْغَايَةَ إِنْ كَانَتْ لِإِسْقَاطِ مَا وَرَاءَ تَدْخُلُ تَحْتَ الْمُغَيَّا وَإِنْ كَانَتْ لِمَدِّ الْحُكْمِ لَا تَدْخُلُ . وَقَالَ زُفَرُ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى لَا يَدْخُلَانِ؛ لِأَنَّ الْغَايَةَ لَا تَدْخُلُ تَحْتَ الْمُغَيَّا كَاللَّيْلِ فِي الصَّوْمِ قُلْنَا نَعَمْ لَكِنْ الْمَرَافِقُ وَالْكَعْبَانِ غَايَةُ إِسْقَاطِ فَلَا يَدْخُلَانِ فِي الْإِسْقَاطِ؛ لِأَنَّ قَوْلَهُ (وَأَيْدِيَكُمْ) (المائدة: ٢) يَتَنَاوَلُ كُلَّ الْأَيْدِى إِلَى الْمَنَاكِبِ فَلَمَّا قَالَ إِلَى الْمَرَافِقِ خَرَجَ مِنْ أَنْ يَكُونَ دَاخِلاَ تَحْتَ السُّقُوطِ ، لأَنَّ الْحَدَّ لَا يَدْخُلُ فِى الْمَحْدُودِ فَبَقِيَ الْغَسْلُ ثَابِتًا فِي الْيَدِ مَعَ الْمَرَافِقِ وَفِي بَابِ الصَّوْمِ لَيْسَتْ الْغَايَةُ غَايَةَ إِسْقَاطِ وَإِنَّمَا هِيَ غَايَةُ امْتِدَادِ الْحُكُم إِلَيْهَا؛ لأنَّ الصَّوْمَ يُطْلَقُ عَلَى الْإِمْسَاكِ سَاعَةً فَهِيَ غَايَةُ إِثْبَاتٍ لَا غَايَةُ إِسْقَاطِ. (الهداية: كتاب الطهارات، ج ا ص ۱۸؛ الجوهرة النيرة: كتاب الطهارة، ج ا ص ۴)
Indeed, the endpoint (ghāyah), if it is for the abrogation (isqāṭ) of what is beyond, enters under the ruled (al-mugheyyā); and if it is for the extension of the ruling, it does not enter. And Zufar (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The two (elbows and ankles) do not enter, because the endpoint does not enter under the ruled, like the night in fasting. We said: Yes, but the elbows and the two ankles are an endpoint of abrogation (ghāyat isqāṭ), so they do not enter under the abrogation; because His statement 'and your hands' (al-Mā'idah 5:6) includes all the hands up to the shoulders. When He said 'to the elbows', they are excluded from being included under the abrogation, because the boundary does not enter into the bounded. Thus the washing remains established in the hand including the elbows. And in the chapter of fasting, the endpoint is not an endpoint of abrogation; rather it is an endpoint of extension of the ruling to it, because fasting is linguistically applied to restraint for even an hour. So it is an endpoint of affirmation (ghāyat ithbāt), not an endpoint of abrogation. (al-Hidayah: Kitāb al-Ṭahārāt; al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah: Kitāb al-Ṭahārah)
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof that also contains an intra-Hanafi dispute. The author first states the general principle: a grammatical endpoint (ghāyah) can be of two types. If it is meant to exclude what is beyond (ghāyat isqāṭ), then the endpoint itself is included in the ruling of the object it limits. If it is meant merely to extend the ruling up to that point but not include it (ghāyat ithbāt), then the endpoint is not included. The author then applies this to the Quranic verse of wudū (al-Mā'idah 5:6), where Allah commands washing the hands 'to the elbows' (ilā al-marāfiq) and the feet 'to the ankles' (ilā al-ka'bayn). The Hanafi position holds that the elbows and ankles must be washed because they are ghāyat isqāṭ: they belong to the same genus as the hand and foot (i.e., parts of the limb), so they are included in the washing obligation, and anything beyond them is excluded. Imam Zufar disagreed, arguing that an endpoint is never included, analogous to the night in the verse of fasting (Sūrat al-Baqarah 2:187) where the night is not part of the fast. The Hanafi response (qulnā) makes the distinction: the elbows and ankles are ghāyat isqāṭ because they are of the same genus, while the night in fasting is ghāyat ithbāt because the night is not of the same genus as fasting (which is defined as abstention from certain acts). Thus, the night does not enter the fasting ruling, but the elbows and ankles enter the washing ruling. This ruling is based on Zahir al-Riwayah (the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybānī) as found in al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
الغاية (al-ghāyah): The endpoint or limit mentioned in a grammatical construction, such as 'to' (ilā) in a ruling.
غاية اسقاط (ghāyat isqāṭ): An endpoint that is of the same genus as the object it limits, thereby being included in the ruling and excluding what is beyond.
غاية اثبات (ghāyat ithbāt): An endpoint that is not of the same genus as the object, serving only to extend the ruling up to that point without including it.
مغيا (al-mugheyyā): The thing that is qualified or limited by the ghāyah, i.e., the object of the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why must the elbows be washed in wudū?
A) Because the verse explicitly says 'wash your hands including the elbows'.
B) Because the elbows are ghāyat isqāṭ and thus included in the ruling for the hand.
C) Because Imam Zufar said so.
D) Because the elbows are of a different genus than the hand.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the rule of ghāyah determines whether an endpoint is included in a ruling based on whether it is of the same genus (ghāyat isqāṭ) or of a different genus (ghāyat ithbāt).

The Principle 'Al-Akthar Hukm al-Kull' Applied to Masah of the Head
Establishes the obligatory measure of masah as quarter of head using the majority principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the obligatory measure of masah and apply the qiyasi reasoning that three fingers represent the whole hand.

Core Idea
For the majority, the ruling of the whole: three fingers suffice for masah because they are the akthar of the hand.

In this passage, Al-Hidayah establishes a key Hanafi principle: 'al-akthar lahu hukm al-kull' (the majority has the ruling of the whole). This principle is applied to determine the obligatory measure of masah (wiping) of the head in wudu. While the Shafi'i school requires wiping the entire head, the Hanafi school holds that wiping a quarter of the head suffices, and this quarter can be achieved by wiping with three fingers. The reasoning is that the hand is the instrument of masah, and the fingers are its primary part; three fingers constitute the majority of the hand's fingers, thus they represent the whole hand by this principle. We will unpack the qiyasi (analogical) reasoning and the textual proofs behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is the masah (wiping) of the head during wudu. The Hanafi school rules that the obligatory (fard) amount is a quarter of the head, not the entire head.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the principle 'al-akthar lahu hukm al-kull'. The passage cites that we are commanded to wipe with the hand, and the fingers are the origin (asl) of the hand. Three fingers are the majority (akthar) of the fingers.
The principle is supported by a ruling on diyah: cutting fingers incurs diyah of the whole hand.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The legal connection: since the command is to wipe with the hand, using the majority of the hand's essential part (the fingers) fulfills the command. The three fingers stand in for the whole hand, thus wiping with three fingers on a quarter of the head suffices.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) with the hand as asl and three fingers as far'.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school and others hold that the entire head must be wiped, arguing from the apparent meaning of the Quranic verse (5:6) 'wamsahu bi ru'usikum' (wipe your heads).
The Hanafi response is that the bi (preposition) indicates a part, not the whole, and the majority principle further restricts that part.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that wiping a quarter of the head with three fingers is obligatory. This is established in Zahir al-Riwayah and supported by later Hanafi authorities.
Tarjih authority: Al-Hidayah and its commentaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲) إِنَّ لِلأَكْثَرِ حُكْمُ الْكُلّ .
ترجمہ: اکثر کے لئے کل کا حکم ہوتا ہے۔
تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ نے اصول مذکورہ ذکر کر کے مسیح راس کی فرض مقدار کا تذکرہ کیا ہے کہ ہمارے ہاں چوتھائی سر کا مسح کرنا فرض ہے نہ کہ مکمل سرکا ، اور اسی اصول پر بعض علماء احناف کا قول پیش کیا ہے، جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ مقدار ناصیہ کی تقدیر تین انگلیاں ہیں ، اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ مسح ہاتھ سے ہوتا ہے اور ہاتھ میں انگلیاں اصل ہیں اور تین انگلیاں اکثر ہیں اور لا كُفَرِ حُكْمُ الكُل " اس قاعدے کے تحت تین انگلیوں کو کل کے قائم مقام بنا کر یہ حکم دیا کہ اگر صرف تین انگلیوں کی مقدار سر کا مسح کیا جائے تو یہ مسح شرعاً کافی ہو جائے گا۔
لِأَنَّا مَأْمُورُونَ بِالْمَسْحِ بِالْيَدِ وَالْأَصَابِعِ أَصْلُهَا وَالثَّلاثَ أَكْثَرُهَا وَلِلَّا كُثَرِ حُكْمُ الْكُلِّ. مجمع الأنهر كتاب الطهارة ، ج ا ص ا ا ، ط: دار إحياء التراث] وتقدير الفرض بثلاثة أصابع الخ أى من أصغر أصابع اليد لأن الأصابع أصل اليد حتى يجب بقطعها دية كل اليد والثلاث أكثرها وللأكثر حكم الكل. [حاشية الـطـحـطـاوى عـلـى مـراقي الفلاح كتاب الطهارة، فصل في أحكام الوضوء، ج ا ص ۶۰، ط: دار الكتب العلمية ] الهداية كتاب الطهارات، ج ۱ ص ۲۲، ط: رحمانیہ
(2) Indeed, for the majority, the ruling of the whole applies.
Translation: The majority has the ruling of the whole.
Explanation: The author of al-Hidayah mentioned the above principle and discussed the obligatory measure of masah of the head: in our school, wiping a quarter of the head is obligatory, not the entire head. Based on this principle, some Hanafi scholars presented an opinion that the measure of the forelock (nasiyah) is three fingers. The reason is that masah is done with the hand, and in the hand, the fingers are the original (asl), and three fingers are the majority. According to the rule 'the majority has the ruling of the whole,' three fingers are made representative of the whole, and it is ruled that if one wipes the head with only three fingers, the masah is legally sufficient.
Because we are commanded to wipe with the hand, and the fingers are its origin, and three are its majority, and the majority has the ruling of the whole. (Majma' al-Anhur, book of Taharah, vol. 1, p. 11) ... And the estimate of the obligatory (masah) is three fingers, i.e., from the smallest fingers of the hand, because the fingers are the origin of the hand, so upon cutting them, the full diyah of the hand is due, and three is the majority, and for the majority, the ruling of the whole applies. (Hashiyat al-Tahtawi 'ala Maraqi al-Falah, book of Taharah, chapter on rulings of wudu, vol. 1, p. 60) Al-Hidayah, book of Taharah, vol. 1, p. 22, Rahmaniyyah edition.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and a qiyasi reasoning regarding the obligatory measure of masah of the head in wudu. The author presents the principle 'al-akthar lahu hukm al-kull' (the majority has the ruling of the whole). This principle is applied to determine that wiping the head with three fingers suffices for the obligatory masah, even though the hand has five fingers. The reasoning: the hand is the instrument of masah, and the fingers are considered the primary (asl) part of the hand. Three fingers constitute the majority (akthar) of the five, and therefore, by this principle, three fingers represent the entire hand for the purpose of fulfilling the obligation. The proof is taken from the Hanafi works: the command to wipe with the hand implies using the hand; the fingers are its core; three is the majority; and the majority carries the ruling of the whole. This is supported by the fact that if fingers are cut, the diyah for the whole hand is due, confirming the fingers' integral status. This ruling aligns with the Hanafi position that the obligatory masah of the head is a quarter of the head, and wiping with three fingers on a quarter fulfills that obligation.

Key Terms
أکثر (akthar): Majority; more than half; in this context, three out of five fingers.
ناصیة (nāṣiyah): Forelock; the front part of the head; used to estimate the obligatory area for masah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The entire head
B) A quarter of the head
C) Three fingers of the head
D) The forelock only

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses the principle 'al-akthar lahu hukm al-kull' to define the obligatory masah as a quarter of the head, represented by wiping with three fingers.

Intention in Wudu: Sunnah, Not Fard
Proves that intention is sunnah in wudu, while wudu without intention is valid as a key to prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that intention in wudu is sunnah (not fard) and explain the proof from Quran 25:48 and the distinction between wudu as a key to prayer and as an act of worship.

Core Idea
Intention in wudu is sunnah, not fard; water purifies by nature, but reward requires intent.

This lesson examines a fundamental difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: is intention (niyyah) obligatory in wudu? The Hanafi position, as presented in al-Hidayah, is that wudu without intention is valid for purification and serves as a key to prayer, but it does not earn reward as an act of worship. The proof lies in the Quranic description of water as inherently purifying.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is intention (niyyah) in wudu. The Hanafi ruling is that intention is sunnah, not fard. The passage states that wudu without intention does not become qurbah (an act of worship), but it does become a key (miftah) for prayer.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the Quranic verse 25:48, which describes water as 'tahur' (purifying). The author argues that the inherent purifying quality of water does not depend on intention.
The verse is: وَأَنْزَلْنَا مِنَ السَّمَاءِ مَاءً طَهُورًا
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The verse does not mention intention as a condition for purification. Therefore, the purifying effect of water (and thus the validity of wudu) is not dependent on intention. This indicates that intention is not obligatory for the validity of wudu.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school holds that intention is fard in wudu, based on the hadith 'Actions are by intentions.' The Hanafi response distinguishes between validity and reward: the hadith concerns reward, not the essential validity of the act of purification.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi law, a person who performs wudu without intention has valid purification and may pray; under Shafi'i law, such wudu is invalid and does not permit prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳) إِنَّ الْوُضُوءَ لَا يَقَعُ قُرُبَةً إِلَّا بِالنِّيَّةِ وَلَكِنَّهُ يَقَعُ مِفْتَاحًا لِلصَّلَاةِ.
ترجمہ: وضو بغیر نیت کے عبادت تو نہیں بن سکتا البتہ نماز کے لئے مفتاح ( آلہ ) کا درجہ رکھتا ہے۔
تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ نے اصول مذکورہ کو ذکر کر کے اس بات کو ثابت کیا ہے کہ احناف کے ہاں وضو میں نیت کرنا سنت ہے نہ کہ فرض ۔ یہ بات مسلّم ہے کہ وضو میں عبادت کا جو وصف ہے وہ بغیر نیت کے متحقق نہیں ہو سکتا، وضو کا ثواب بغیر نیت کے حاصل نہیں ہوتا لیکن وضو بغیر نیت کے بھی مفتاح صلاۃ بن سکتا ہے، اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ نماز کی مفتاح طہارت ہے اور طہارت کے لئے نیت ضروری نہیں ہے، اس لئے کہ پانی اپنی ذات کے اعتبار سے پاک کرنے والا ہے، جیسا کہ اللہ تعالی نے ارشاد فرمایا وَأَنْزَلْنَا مِنَ السَّمَاءِ مَاءً طَهُورًا " ( الفرقان: ۴۸) اس آیت میں اللہ تعالی نے طہارت کے لئے نیت کا ذکر نہیں کیا، لہذا پانی کو جب بھی کوئی شخص وضو وغیرہ کے لئے استعمال کرے گا اور وضو کے فرائض اربعہ کی تکمیل کرے گا تو بلا شبہ اس شخص کا وضو متحقق ہو جائے گا، خواہ نیت کرے یا نہ کرے، لہذا معلوم ہوا کہ وضو میں نیت کرنا سنت ہے نہ کہ فرض ۔
وَأَمَّا الْمَاء فَمُطَهِّرٌ بِطَبْعِهِ فَلَا يَحْتَاجُ إلَى النِّيَّةِ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ لَا يَقَعُ قُرْبَةً بِدُونِ النِّيَّةِ لَكِنَّهُ يَقَعُ مِفْتَاحًا لِلصَّلَاةِ لِوُقُوعِهِ طَهَارَةً باسْتِعْمَال الْمَاء الْمُطَهِّر. الجوهرة النيرة : كتاب الطهارة ، سنن الطهارة ، ج ا ص ۷، ط: المطبعة الخيرية ] فإنه لا يحصل له ثواب الوضوء بدون النية، إذ الثواب لا يحصل إلا بالقربة ولا يقع قربة إلا بالنية عندنا أيضاً، ولكنه يقع مفتاحاً للصلاة بدونها. الغرة المنيفة في تحقيق بعض مسائل الإمام أبي حنيفة: ص ۲۰، ط: مؤسسة الكتب
(3) Indeed, wudu does not become an act of worship (qurbah) except with intention, but it serves as a key (mithah) for prayer.
Translation: Wudu cannot become an act of worship without intention; however, it has the status of a key (instrument) for prayer.
Explanation: The author of al-Hidayah mentioned the above principle and proved that in the Hanafi school, intention in wudu is sunnah, not fard. It is established that the quality of worship in wudu cannot be realized without intention; the reward of wudu is not obtained without intention. But wudu without intention can still be a key for prayer. The reason is that the key to prayer is purification (taharah), and intention is not necessary for purification because water is purifying by its essence, as Allah said: 'And We sent down from the sky pure water' (Al-Furqan 25:48). In this verse, Allah did not mention intention for purification. Therefore, whenever a person uses water for wudu etc. and completes the four fard acts of wudu, his wudu is undoubtedly valid, whether he intends or not. Thus it is known that intention in wudu is sunnah, not fard.
And as for water, it is purifying by its nature, so it does not need intention. However, it does not become an act of worship without intention, but it serves as a key for prayer because it becomes purification by using purifying water. (Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah, book of Taharah, chapter on sunan of Taharah, vol. 1, p. 7) For he does not obtain the reward of wudu without intention, since reward is only obtained through an act of worship, and it does not become an act of worship except with intention in our school as well. But it becomes a key to prayer without it. (Al-Ghurar al-Munifah fi Tahqiq Ba'd Masa'il al-Imam Abi Hanifah, p. 20)
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the status of intention (niyyah) in wudu according to the Hanafi school. The author states the principle: wudu without intention is not an act of worship (qurbah) deserving reward, but it is valid as a means (miftah) to perform prayer. The author then explains that the Hanafi position is that intention is sunnah, not fard. The proof is that water is inherently purifying (mutahhir bi-tab'ihi), as per the Quranic verse (25:48) which does not condition purification on intention. Therefore, if a person performs the four fard acts of wudu (washing face, washing arms, wiping head, washing feet) without intending wudu, the wudu is valid and one may pray with it. However, to gain the reward (thawab) of wudu, intention is required because reward is only for acts of worship, and an act is only worship with intention. This distinction shows that wudu without intention is sufficient for ritual purity but deficient in merit. This position is opposed to the Shafi'i school, which considers intention in wudu as obligatory (fard). The references from Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah and Al-Ghurar al-Munifah confirm this Hanafi stance.

Key Terms
نیت (niyyah): Intention; the inner resolve to perform an act of worship for Allah's sake.
مفتاح (miftāh): Key; here it means a means or prerequisite: wudu without intention serves as a key to prayer by providing ritual purity.
قربت (qurbah): An act of worship that brings one closer to Allah; requires intention for reward.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on intention (niyyah) in wudu?
A) Fard
B) Wajib
C) Sunnah
D) Makruh

Key Takeaway
Intention in wudu is sunnah, not fard; wudu without intention is valid as a key to prayer, but reward depends on intention.

Wiping the Head with Three Fingers
Ruling and proof for wiping head with three fingers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the minimum hand measure for wiping the head in wudu and explain the legal basis using the maxim 'al-akthar lahu hukm al-kull'.

Core Idea
Three fingers suffice for head wiping because they are the majority of the hand, which is the origin of wiping.

This lesson addresses a key question in wudu: how much of the hand must be used when wiping the head? The Hanafi school teaches that wiping with three fingers is sufficient, based on a clear proof and a well-established legal maxim. Understanding this ruling helps distinguish the obligatory measure from mere custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is wiping (masah) the head during wudu. The ruling states that wiping with the measure of three fingers is legally sufficient, meaning it fulfills the obligation.
The obligation itself is to wipe a quarter of the head; the three fingers refer to the minimum hand contact required.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the command to wipe with the hand (yad). The fingers are the essential part (asl) of the hand, so wiping with the hand necessarily involves wiping with the fingers.
The command is found in the Quran and Sunnah; the text references 'we are commanded' (ma'murun).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative to wipe with the hand indicates that the hand is the instrument. The fingers are the core of the hand, and three fingers constitute the majority (akthar) of the five fingers. The legal maxim 'al-akthar lahu hukm al-kull' (the majority has the ruling of the whole) applies, so three fingers stand for the entire hand.
This reasoning is specific to the Hanafi school; other schools may interpret the evidence differently.
4
Note cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school holds that a single touch with any part of the hand suffices, while the Hanafi require at least three fingers. This difference stems from varying interpretations of the hand's role and the application of the majority maxim.
The Hanafi position relies on the maxim; Shafi'is do not accept that maxim in this context.
5
Confirm the reliance on the maxim
The passage explicitly cites the rule 'li'l-akthari hukm al-kull'. This maxim is widely used in Hanafi fiqh to extend rulings from the majority to the whole.
The commentary from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi reinforces this by noting that if all fingers are cut, full diyah is due, showing their integral status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انگلیوں کی مقدار سر کا مسح کیا جائے تو یہ مسح شرعاً کافی ہو جائے گا۔ لِأَنَّا مَأْمُورُونَ بِالْمَسْحِ بِالْيَدِ وَالْأَصَابِعِ أَصْلُهَا وَالثَّلاثَ أَكْثَرُهَا وَلِلْأَكْثَرِ حُكْمُ الْكُلِّ. [مجمع الأنهر كتاب الطهارة ، ج ا ص ا ا ، ط: دار إحياء التراث] وتقدير الفرض بثلاثة أصابع الخ أى من أصغر أصابع اليد لأن الأصابع أصل اليد حتى يجب بقطعها دية كل اليد والثلاث أكثرها وللأكثر حكم الكل. [حاشية الـطـحـطـاوى عـلـى مـراقي الفلاح كتاب الطهارة، فصل في أحكام الوضوء، ج ا ص ۶۰، ط: دار الكتب العلمية ]
If the head is wiped with the measure of the fingers, this wiping will be legally sufficient. Because we are commanded to wipe with the hand and the fingers are its origin, and three is the majority of them, and the majority has the ruling of the whole. [Majma' al-Anhur, Kitab al-Taharah] And the estimation of the obligation is with three fingers, i.e., from the smallest fingers of the hand because the fingers are the origin of the hand, such that if they are cut off, the full diyah of the hand is wajib, and three is the majority, and the majority has the ruling of the whole.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage states a ruling regarding the wiping of the head in wudu: it is sufficient to wipe with the measure of the fingers, specifically three fingers. The proof is that we are commanded to wipe with the hand, and the fingers are the essential part of the hand. Since three fingers constitute the majority of the hand, the ruling of the whole is applied to them, following the legal maxim 'al-akthar lahu hukm al-kull' (the majority has the ruling of the whole). The commentary from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi explains that the three fingers should be from the smallest fingers (of the hand) because the fingers are the origin of the hand; if all fingers are cut, the full diyah (blood money) for the hand is due. As three is the majority, it stands for the whole.

Key Terms
مسح (masah): Wiping a limb with water, as part of wudu, without letting it flow.
أصابع (asabi'): Fingers of the hand; the smallest fingers are intended for the measure of wiping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many fingers must be used when wiping the head in wudu?
A) One finger
B) Two fingers
C) Three fingers
D) All five fingers

Key Takeaway
Three fingers suffice for wiping the head in Hanafi wudu because they are the majority of the hand, applying the maxim that the majority has the ruling of the whole.

Repetition Harms Wiping but Not Washing
Ruling that repeating an action harms masah but not ghusl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on repetition in wudu for washing and wiping, and explain why repetition harms masah but not ghusl.

Core Idea
Repeating washing in wudu is harmless; repeating wiping breaks it.

In this lesson, we learn a key distinction in the acts of wudu: the effect of repeating an action. While repetition is harmless for washing limbs, the same is not true for wiping the head. This ruling reflects a deeper legal principle about the nature of each act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two actions
Recognize that the ruling discusses two distinct acts: ghusl (washing) and masah (wiping). Ghusl refers to washing the face, hands, and feet; masah refers to wiping the head.
Ensure you know which parts of wudu involve washing and which involve wiping.
2
State the rule for ghusl
The ruling clearly states that repetition does not harm washing. This means if you accidentally or deliberately wash a limb more than once, the wudu remains valid.
The Hanafi school considers it permissible to wash more than once; the sunnah is thrice.
3
State the rule for masah
The ruling states that repetition does harm wiping. This means if you wipe your head more than once deliberately and knowingly, the masah becomes invalid, and consequently the whole wudu is invalid.
The Hanafi school holds that wiping should be done exactly once; any additional wipe breaks the act.
4
Understand the rationale
Washing is intended for cleansing; repetition aids cleanliness. Wiping is symbolic and minimal; repetition goes against its purpose and is considered an innovation.
This principle is derived from the general objectives of shari'ah (maqasid).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٥) إِنَّ التَّكْرَارَ لَا يَضُرُّ الْغَسْلَ بِخِلَافِ الْمَسْحِ فَإِنَّهُ يَضُرُّهُ. ترجمہ: تکرار غسل کے لئے مضر نہیں ہے بخلاف مسح کے اس کے لئے تکرار مضر ہے۔ تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ نے اصول مذکورہ کے ذریعہ اس بات کو ثابت کیا ہے کہ غسل میں الهداية كتاب الطهارات، ج ۱ ص ۲۳، ط: رحمانیه
(5) Indeed, repetition does not harm washing, contrary to wiping, for it harms it. Translation: Repetition is not harmful for washing, unlike wiping, for it harms it. Commentary: The author of al-Hidayah used the aforementioned principle to prove that in washing... (cut off). Reference: al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Taharah, vol. 1, p. 23, Rahmaniyya edition.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage states a ruling about the effect of repetition (takrar) in the acts of wudu: repeating the washing (ghusl) of a limb is not harmful and is permissible, whereas repeating the wiping (masah) of the head is harmful and can invalidate the wudu or require compensation. The commentary from al-Hidayah is referenced but incomplete here. The underlying juristic reasoning is that washing is meant to clean, and repetition aids cleaning, while wiping is a symbolic act that should be done once; repeating it is against its intended purpose.

Key Terms
غسل (ghusl): Washing a limb with water, making the water flow over it. In wudu, the washing of the face, hands, and feet.
مسح (masah): Wiping a part of the body with wet hand, e.g., wiping the head in wudu. It is not a flow of water.
تکرار (takrar): Repeating the same action (washing or wiping) twice or more during wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is recommended.
B) It is permissible but not harmful.
C) It is harmful and invalidates the wudu.
D) It is obligatory to repeat three times.

Key Takeaway
Repetition in washing is harmless; repetition in wiping invalidates wudu.

Earth Is Not Inherently Purifying: Intention in Tayammum vs. Wudu
Inter-madhhab dispute on intention in wudu vs. tayammum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on intention in wudu and tayammum, explain the refutation of the Shafi'i analogy, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Earth purifies only by divine command requiring intention; water purifies by nature, thus wudu needs no intention.

This passage from al-Hidayah establishes a key principle differentiating wudu and tayammum. It is presented to rebut Imam al-Shafi'i's analogy that intention is required in both. Understanding this principle clarifies the Hanafi view on devotional acts and the limits of qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Read the opening statement: 'Earth is not a purifier except in the state of intending prayer.' This is the core principle: earth (turab) is not inherently purifying (mutahhir).
Contrast with water, which is inherently purifying.
2
Understand the Shafi'i qiyas
Imam al-Shafi'i analogizes wudu to tayammum: since tayammum requires niyyah, wudu should also require niyyah. Identify the asl (tayammum) and far' (wudu) in this analogy.
Qiyas requires a shared illa; the Hanafi school disputes this.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response rejects the analogy because the illa differs: water is mutahhir bi-dhatihi (purifying by itself), while earth is not. Earth's purification is ta'abbudi (purely devotional), so it needs intention. Therefore, wudu does not require intention.
This is a classic example of difference in illa breaking qiyas.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a person who performs wudu without intention has a valid wudu in the Hanafi school but invalid in the Shafi'i school. For tayammum, both agree intention is required, but the reasoning differs.
This distinction is crucial for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٢) إِنَّ التَّرَابَ غَيْرُ مُطَهِّرِ إِلَّا فِي حَالَ إِرَادَةِ الصَّلَاةِ. ترجمہ مٹی پاک کرنے والی نہیں ہے مگر ارادہ صلاۃ کی حالت میں۔ تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ اصول مذکورہ کو امام شافعی رحمہ اللہ کے ذکر کردہ اس قیاس کے رد میں لائے ہیں جو انہوں نے وضو کو تیم پر قیاس کیا ہے کہ جس طرح تیم میں نیت کرنا ضروری ہے اسی طرح وضو میں بھی نیت کرنا ضروری ہے۔ امام شافعی رحمہ اللہ کا وضو و تیم پر قیاس کرنا درست نہیں ، اس لئے کہ وضو پانی سے کیا جاتا ہے اور پانی بذاتہ پاک کرنے والا ہے، اور تیم مٹی سے ہوتا ہے اور مٹی بذاتہ پاک کرنے والی نہیں بلکہ یہ جسم کو ملوث اور مغیر کرنے والی ہے مگر یہ کہ ارادہ صلاۃ کی حالت میں۔ پانی کے نہ ملنے کی صورت میں ارادہ نماز کے وقت خلاف قیاس مٹی کو مظہر بنا دیا جاتا ہے، جسے فقہاء کرام کی اصطلاح میں امر تعبدی کہا جاتا ہے ، امر تعبدی نیت کے محتاج ہوتے ہیں بغیر نیت کے امیر تعبدی کا متحقق نہیں ہوتا ، اس لئے تیم میں نیت کرنا ضروری ہے لیکن اس ضرورت کو وضو کے لئے لازم نہیں قرار دیا جاسکتا، لہذا وضو کو تیم پر قیاس کر کے یہ حکم لگانا کہ جس طرح تیم میں نیت کرنا ضروری ہے اسی طرح وضو میں بھی نیت کرنا ضروری ہے یہ درست نہیں۔ بِخِلَافِ التَّيَمُّم فَإِنَّ التُّرَابَ لَمْ يُعْقَلُ مُطَهِّرًا طَبْعًا فَلَمْ يَبْقَ فِيهِ إِلَّا مَعْنَى التَّعَبدِ وَلَا تَعَبُّدَ بِدُونِ النِّيَّةِ. العناية شرح الهداية كتاب الطهارة، ج ۱ ص ۳۳، ط: دار الفکر) لأن التراب غير مطهر) يعنى لم يعقل مطهرا؛ لأنه فى ذاته ملوث ومغير فلا يكون مطهرا إلا في حالة إرادة الصلاة فتكون طهارته بدلا عن الوضوء ، لأنه بطبعه وحقيقته مطهر. البناية شرح الهداية كتاب الطهارة ، السنية فى الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۲۳۶، ط: دار الكتب العلمية الهداية كتاب الطهارات، ج ۱ ص ۲۳، ط: رحمانیه
(2) Verily, earth is not a purifier except in the state of intending prayer. Translation: Earth is not purifying except when intending prayer. Explanation: The author of al-Hidayah brings this principle to refute the analogy made by Imam al-Shafi'i, who analogized wudu to tayammum, saying that just as intention is necessary in tayammum, so too is intention necessary in wudu. Imam al-Shafi'i's analogy of wudu to tayammum is not correct, because wudu is done with water, and water is inherently purifying, while tayammum is done with earth, and earth is not inherently purifying; rather, it is defiling and altering, except when intending prayer. In the absence of water, at the time of intending prayer, contrary to analogy, earth is made a means, which jurists call a devotional act (amr ta'abbudi). Devotional acts require intention; without intention, a devotional act is not realized. Therefore, intention is necessary in tayammum, but this necessity cannot be imposed on wudu. Hence, analogizing wudu to tayammum to rule that intention is necessary in wudu as in tayammum is incorrect. Contrary to tayammum, because earth is not rationally understood as a purifier by nature, so nothing remains in it except the meaning of devotion, and there is no devotion without intention. (Al-'Inayah) 'Because earth is not a purifier' meaning it is not rationally understood as a purifier; because in itself it is defiling and altering, so it is not a purifier except in the state of intending prayer, thus its purification is a substitute for wudu, because water by its nature and reality is a purifier. (Al-Binayah) Al-Hidayah, Book of Purifications, vol. 1, p. 23, Rahmaniyyah.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational principle from al-Hidayah: earth is not inherently purifying; it becomes purifying only when one intends to pray (i.e., performs tayammum as a substitute for wudu due to lack of water). The principle is used to refute Imam al-Shafi'i's analogy that since tayammum requires intention, wudu must also require intention. The Hanafi response is clear: water is naturally purifying (mutahhir bi-dhatihi), so wudu is valid without intention (though intention is sunnah). Earth, by contrast, is not purifying by nature; its purifying quality is purely devotional (ta'abbudi) and therefore depends on intention. Analogy fails because the basis (asl) and branch (far') differ in the essential attribute of inherent purifying power. The Arabic commentary from 'Inayah and Binayah reinforces this: earth is rationally understood as non-purifying except by divine command in the case of prayer intention.

Key Terms
التَّرَاب (al-turaab): Earth or dust used for tayammum.
مُطَهِّر (mutahhir): Something that purifies (ritually) by its own nature.
إِرَادَة الصَّلَاة (iraadat al-salaah): The intention to perform prayer, which activates the purifying quality of earth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school require intention (niyyah) in tayammum but not in wudu?
A) Because tayammum is performed without water
B) Because earth is not inherently purifying; it is a devotional act requiring intention
C) Because water is scarce in most situations
D) Because the Quran specifically mentions intention for tayammum

Key Takeaway
Earth is not inherently purifying; its purifying effect in tayammum is a devotional act requiring intention, unlike water in wudu.

Repetition Harms Wiping but Not Washing
Principle differentiating washing and wiping regarding repetition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that repetition (takrar) is sunnah in washing but harmful in wiping, and apply it to distinguish the Hanafi and Shafi'i rulings on wiping the head.

Core Idea
Repetition (three times) is sunnah in washing but harmful in wiping, changing its nature.

In wudu and ghusl, washing limbs three times is a confirmed sunnah. However, for wiping (mash) the head or khuffs, the Hanafi school holds that repeating the act is not sunnah and can actually harm the validity of the wiping. This principle, grounded in the nature of the acts, serves as a key response to the Shafi'i analogy that wiping should also be done three times. In this lesson, we will unpack the reasoning behind this distinction and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The principle: repetition (specifically three times) is sunnah in washing (ghasl) but harmful (yadurru) in wiping (mash). For Hanafis, wiping the head is fard once; repeating it is makruh tanzihi and may turn wiping into washing, which is not the prescribed act.
The harm lies in altering the nature of the act from mash to ghusl.
2
Identify the opposing view and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i argues by qiyas: since washing limbs is done three times (sunnah), wiping the head should also be done three times. This is an analogy based on similarity.
Shafi'is consider the head a limb of wudu, so they analogize it to the washed limbs.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes the acts: washing benefits from repetition (more cleanliness), while wiping does not. Repetition in wiping turns it into washing, violating the Quranic command 'wip your heads' (Quran 5:6). Furthermore, wiping the head is analogous to wiping khuffs, where repetition is not sunnah.
The analogy to khuffs breaks the Shafi'i qiyas.
4
Determine the practical difference
A Hanafi who wipes his head three times with the intention of sunnah performs a makruh act and may have to repeat wudu if the wiping effectively becomes washing. A Shafi'i considers it sunnah and valid.
The thamarah al-khilaf: actual practice differs in wudu performance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۵) إِنَّ التَّكْرَارَ لَا يَضُرُّ الْغَسْلَ بِخِلَافِ الْمَسْحِ فَإِنَّهُ يَضُرُّهُ. ترجمہ: تکرار غسل کے لئے مضر نہیں ہے بخلاف مسح کے اس کے لئے تکرار مضر ہے۔ تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ نے اصول مذکورہ کے ذریعہ اس بات کو ثابت کیا ہے کہ غسل میں احناف کے ہاں تکرار مسنون ہے لیکن مسح میں تکرار نہیں ہے۔ اس اصول کو ایک مثال کے ذریعہ سے واضح کیا ہے (اصول مذکورہ امام شافعی رحمہ اللہ کے قیاس کا جواب بھی ہے جو انہوں نے سر کے مسح کو اعضاء مغسولہ پر قیاس کیا)۔ جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ سر کا مسح کرنا فرض ہے نہ کہ دھونا ، اب اگر مسح میں تثلیث اور تکرار کو مان لیا جائے اور اعضاء مغسولہ کی طرح تین مرتبہ مسح کیا جائے تو اس صورت میں یہ سح مسح نہیں رہے گا بلکہ تکرار کی وجہ سے غسل بن جائے گا، جبکہ قرآن کریم میں مسح کا حکم ہے۔ سر کا مسح موزے کے مسح کے مشابہ ہے، جس طرح موزے کے مسیح میں تثلیث اور تکرار مسنون نہیں ، اسی طرح سر کے مسح میں بھی تکرار مسنون نہیں ہو گا لیکن اس کے برخلاف غسل میں تکرار اور تثلیث مسنون ہے کیونکہ تکرار فسل کے لئے مفید ہے اس سے مزید نظافت اور پاکیزگی حاصل ہوتی ہے، جبکہ مسح کے لئے تکرار مضر ہے۔
(5) Verily, repetition does not harm washing, contrary to wiping, for it harms it. Translation: Repetition is not harmful for washing, unlike wiping; repetition is harmful for it. Explanation: The author of al-Hidayah, through this principle, establishes that, among the Hanafis, repetition (three times) is sunnah in washing, but in wiping there is no repetition. This principle is clarified with an example (and it is also a response to Imam al-Shafi'i's analogy, who analogized wiping the head to washing the limbs). The gist is that wiping the head is fard, not washing. Now, if repetition and three times are accepted in wiping, and one wipes three times like the washed limbs, then this act would no longer be wiping but would become washing, whereas the Quran commands wiping. Wiping the head resembles wiping the leather socks (khuff); just as in wiping the socks, three times and repetition are not sunnah, similarly in wiping the head, repetition is not sunnah. But in contrast, in washing, repetition and three times are sunnah because repetition is beneficial for washing—it brings more cleanliness and purity—while for wiping, repetition is harmful.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents a key Hanafi principle: repetition (specifically doing an act three times) is recommended (sunnah) in washing (ghusl and wudu limbs) but not in wiping (massh of head or khuff). In fact, repetition harms the validity of wiping because repeated wiping would transform the act into washing, which is not what the Quran prescribed for the head (Quran 5:6 commands 'wip your heads'). The principle is used to refute Imam al-Shafi'i's analogy that since washing limbs is done three times (sunnah), wiping the head should also be done three times. The Hanafi response: the acts differ in nature—washing benefits from repetition (more purity), wiping does not; repetition turns wiping into washing, which violates the Quranic command. The head is analogized to the leather socks (khuff), where repetition is also not sunnah. Thus, the Hanafi position is that wiping the head is fard once, and repeating it is not recommended (makruh).

Key Terms
التَّكْرَار (al-takrar): Repeating an act, here specifically doing it three times (tathlith).
الغَسْل (al-ghasl): Washing with water, i.e., pouring water over a limb.
المسح (al-mash): Wiping, i.e., passing wet hand over a body part (head, khuff).

D) It is recommended but not required

Key Takeaway
Repetition is sunnah in washing but harmful in wiping—a key Hanafi distinction rooted in the nature of the acts and the Quranic command.

The Letter Waw Indicates Absolute Conjunction: Why Tartib in Wudu Is Sunnah
Principle establishing that waw does not imply order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that order (tartib) in wudu is sunnah, not fard, based on the linguistic principle of waw, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Waw means 'and', not 'then' — order in wudu is sunnah, not fard.

This lesson explores a critical linguistic principle that shapes a key difference between Hanafi and Shafi'i fiqh: the meaning of the letter waw in the wudu verse. By examining the consensus of Arabic linguists, we see how the Hanafi school derives that performing wudu limbs in sequence is recommended (sunnah), not obligatory (fard). Understanding this principle is essential for grasping Hanafi usul and the school's distinctive approach to textual interpretation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school rules order (tartib) in wudu as sunnah muakkadah, not fard. Note that this differs from the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali positions.
2
Identify the linguistic proof
Examine the principle: 'Inna al-waw li-mutlaq al-jam' bi-ijma' ahl al-lughah.' The waw in the wudu verse (Quran 5:6) indicates only conjunction, not sequence.
This is a qat'i dalalah (definitive meaning) based on consensus.
3
Examine the Shafi'i objection
The opposing school argues that the Prophet's consistent practice of order, combined with the verse's context, makes tartib fard. Understand their dalil.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafis respond that waw's meaning is decisive; the Prophet's practice shows sunnah, not fard. The difference between fard and sunnah is established by other proofs (e.g., the lack of explicit command for order).
The thamarah al-khilaf: wudu without tartib is valid in Hanafi but invalid in Shafi'i.

(2) Verily, the letter waw is for absolute conjunction by consensus of the linguists. Translation: The letter waw, according to the unanimous decision of linguists, is used for absolute conjunction. Explanation: The author of al-Hidayah mentions this principle to establish that, among the Hanafis, order (tartib) in wudu is sunnah, not fard, as is the position of the three Imams (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad). In the verse 'So wash your faces and your hands...' (Quran 5:6), the letter waw appears between the limbs of wudu, and it indicates absolute conjunction, not order. Therefore, the limbs... And its meaning is that repetition spoils wiping, unlike washing, which it does not spoil; thus al-Shafi'i's analogy of the wiped to the washed is invalid. (Al-'Inayah: 'Repetition spoils wiping, unlike washing; it does not spoil it but increases its cleanliness and purification, so al-Shafi'i's analogy of the wiped to the washed is invalid.' Al-Binayah: '...' Al-Hidayah, Book of Purifications, vol. 1, p. 23, Rahmaniyyah.)
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents a linguistic principle: the conjunction 'waw' in Arabic denotes absolute collection or conjunction (mujarrar al-jam'), not sequential order. This is by consensus of Arabic linguists. The author of al-Hidayah uses this principle to argue that in the wudu verse (Quran 5:6), the waw between 'faces' and 'hands' etc. does not require performing these acts in sequence. Therefore, order (tartib) in wudu is not fard in the Hanafi school; it is sunnah (recommended). This refutes the position of the Shafi'i and other imams who consider order fard. The passage then includes a brief reference back to the previous principle concerning repetition, emphasizing that the whole discussion validates the Hanafi stance. The Hanafi position is that if one washes the limbs out of order, the wudu is still valid (though it is better to follow order).

Key Terms
الْوَاو (al-waw): The Arabic conjunction meaning 'and'.
مُطْلَقِ الْجَمْعِ (mutlaq al-jam'): Absolute conjunction or collection without any implication of order.
التَّرْتِيب (al-tartib): Sequence or order in performing the acts of wudu.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the waw in the wudu verse indicates absolute conjunction, so order is sunnah, not fard.

Repetition in Wiping vs Washing: A Hanafi Principle
Explaining that repetition invalidates masah but benefits ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that repetition (takrar) invalidates masah but is recommended in ghusl, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Repetition transforms masah into ghusl; since the Quran commands masah, repetition invalidates it.

In this lesson, we examine a key principle in Hanafi fiqh: repetition (takrar) is beneficial for washing but harmful for wiping. This principle forms the basis of the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i analogy equating head wiping to limb washing. We will analyze the ruling, the proofs, and the practical difference between the schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is being discussed? The act of wiping (masah) of the head in wudu. Determine the Hanafi ruling on repeating this act.
The text states: 'al-masah yufsiduhu al-takrar' – repetition invalidates wiping.
2
State the Hanafi position
Hanafis hold that repeating masah is not sunnah; it invalidates the wipe because it changes the act into washing (ghusl).
3
State the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i analogized masah of the head to ghusl of limbs, arguing that threefold repetition is mustahabb.
Shafi'is consider triple wiping as recommended, as in washing.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: the analogy is invalid because repetition changes the nature of the act. Masah is not for cleaning like ghusl; it is a symbolic act. Repeating it turns it into washing, contravening the Quranic command of masah.
Also, qiyas on masah of khuff (leather socks) – no repetition recommended.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Shafi'is consider threefold wiping as mustahabb (rewardable); Hanafis consider a single wipe sufficient and repetition disliked/invalidating.
Mufta bihi in Hanafi school: one wipe, no repetition.

Key Terms

تکرار (takrar): Repeating the act (e.g., washing or wiping) multiple times.
قياس (qiyas): Legal analogy; extrapolating a ruling from an original case to a new case based on a common effective cause (illah).

The Waw and Order in Wudu: Sunnah Not Fard
Proving that order in wudu is sunnah not fard based on linguistic analysis of waw

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on order in wudu (tarteeb) and explain the linguistic proof from the letter waw.

Core Idea
The waw in the Quran means conjunction without sequence — so order in wudu is sunnah, not fard.

This passage from Al-Hidayah tackles a key inter-madhhab dispute: is order in wudu obligatory (fard) or merely recommended (sunnah)? The answer turns on the meaning of the Arabic letter waw (and). The Hanafis argue that waw indicates absolute conjunction, not sequence, so the Quran does not mandate order. However, the Sunnah establishes order as a confirmed practice. Let's examine the proof and the rebuttal to objections.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafis rule order in wudu as sunnah mu'akkadah, not fard. Find the statement in the passage that confirms this.
See the commentary: 'order in wudu is a confirmed sunnah'.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The three other Imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that order is fard. The passage mentions this as the contrasting view.
These are the 'three Imams' referenced.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponents might rely on the consistent practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) or interpret the waw as implying order in some contexts. The passage addresses this by citing linguistic consensus.
The passage does not detail their proof, but we infer it.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis invoke the consensus of linguists (e.g., Abu Ali al-Farisi, Sibawayh) that waw is for absolute conjunction. They address the objection that some linguists disagree by noting that minority dissent does not negate consensus.
The author of Al-Hidayah relies on this linguistic ijma.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if a person intentionally reverses the order (e.g., washes feet before face), the wudu is valid according to Hanafis (but makruh), while it would be invalid according to the majority.
This difference affects daily practice.

ترجمہ : حرف واو اہل لغت کے متفقہ فیصلے کی وجہ سے مطلق جمع کے لئے آتا ہے۔
تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ نے اصول مذکورہ کو ذکر کر کے اس بات کو ثابت کیا ہے کہ احناف کے ہاں وضو میں ترتیب مسنون ہے نہ کہ فرض جیسا کہ حضرات ائمہ ثلاثہ کا مسلک ہے۔ فَاغْسِلُوا وُجُوهَكُمْ وَأَيْدِيَكُمُ “ اس آیت میں ارکانِ وضو کے درمیان حرف و او آیا ہے، جو مطلق جمع کے لئے آتا ہے، ترتیب کے لئے نہیں آتا، اس لئے اعضائے مفروضہ کے
درمیان ترتیب فرض نہیں ہے، مگر چونکہ احادیث مبارکہ میں قرآنی ترتیب کے مطابق وضو کا ذکر ہے اس لئے وضو میں ترتیب سنتِ مؤکدہ ہے۔
وَإِنَّمَا الْكَلَامُ فِي تَرْتِيبِ الْأَعْضَاءِ ، وَالدَّاخِلُ فِيهَا الْوَاوُ وَهِيَ لَا تُفِيدُ التَّرْتِيبَ، فَإِنْ قِيلَ: كَيْفَ ادَّعَى الْمُصَنِّفُ إِجْمَاعَ أَهْلِ اللُّغَةِ وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ يَقُولُ إِنَّهُ يُفِيدُ التَّرْتِيبَ وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ يَقُولُ يُفِيدُ الْقِرَانَ، أُجِيبَ بِأَنَّ أَبَا عَلَى الْفَارِسِيَّ ذَكَرَ أَنَّ التَّحَاةَ أَجْمَعُوا أَنَّ الْوَاوَ لِلْجَمْعِ الْمُطْلَقِ، ذَكَرَهُ سِيبَوَيْهِ فِى سَبْعَةَ عَشَرَ مَوْضِعًا فِي كِتَابِهِ فَاعْتَمَدَ الْمُصَنِّفُ عَلَى ذَلِكَ وَبِأَنَّ خِلَافَ الْقَلِيلِ لَا يَمْنَعُ الْإِجْمَاعَ اللُّغَوِيَّ.
العناية كتاب الطهارات ج ۱ ص ۳۵، ط: دار الفكر "الترتيب "سنة مؤكدة في الصحيح وهو كما نص الله تعالى في كتابه ولم يكن فرضا لأن الواو في الأمر لمطلق الجمع والفاء التى فى قوله تعالى (فَاغْسِلُوا) لتعقيب جملة الأعضاء .
مراقی الفلاح فصل فى سنن الوضوء، ص ۳۴، ط: المكتبة العصرية ]
الهداية كتاب الطهارات، ج ۱ ص ۲۴، ط: رحمانیه
(2) Indeed the letter waw is for absolute conjunction by consensus of the linguists. Translation: The letter waw comes for absolute conjunction due to the unanimous decision of the linguists. Explanation: The author of al-Hidayah has mentioned this principle to prove that according to the Hanafis order in wudu is sunnah, not fard, contrary to the three Imams. In the verse 'Wash your faces and your hands', the letter waw appears between the pillars of wudu, which indicates absolute conjunction, not order. Therefore order among the prescribed limbs is not fard, but because the blessed hadith mention wudu in the Quranic order, order in wudu is a confirmed sunnah. And the discussion is about the order of the limbs, and the waw that enters them does not indicate order. If it is said: How did the author claim consensus of the linguists when some say it indicates order and some say it indicates association? The answer: Abu Ali al-Farisi mentioned that the linguists agreed that waw is for absolute conjunction. Sibawayh mentioned it in seventeen places in his book, so the author relied on that, and the minority disagreement does not negate linguistic consensus.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, specifically a dispute with the majority of scholars (the three Imams) who hold that order (tarteeb) in wudu is obligatory (fard). The Hanafis derive their position from the linguistic principle that the letter waw (and) indicates absolute conjunction, not sequence. In the Quranic verse (5:6), the limbs are connected with waw, so the order is not mandatory. However, because the Sunnah consistently presents the order, it becomes a confirmed sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah). The author responds to an objection about the claim of consensus, citing Abu Ali al-Farisi and Sibawayh to affirm the linguistic consensus, stating that rare dissent does not negate it. The Arabic quotes from al-Inayah and Maraqi al-Falah reinforce that tarteeb is sunnah, not fard.

Key Terms
واو (waw): The Arabic letter 'and' used as a conjunction between items of a list.
مطلق الجمع (mutlaq al-jam'): Absolute conjunction: connecting items without implying any order or sequence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis consider order (tarteeb) in wudu as sunnah rather than fard?
A) Because the hadith explicitly state it is optional
B) Because the Quran uses the letter waw which indicates conjunction, not sequence
C) Because washing the face first is not mentioned in the Quran
D) Because the four schools are in disagreement

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling that order in wudu is sunnah rests on the linguistic principle that waw indicates absolute conjunction, supported by consensus of linguists; the order is confirmed by the Sunnah.

The Word 'Ma' Indicates Generality in Nullifiers of Wudu
Establishing that anything exiting the two passages breaks wudu, based on the general word 'ma'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on nullifiers of wudu, cite the linguistic proof from the word 'ma', and distinguish the Maliki view on habitual vs non-habitual exits.

Core Idea
The word 'ma' in 'ma yakhruju min al-sabilayn' is general — any exit, habitual or not, nullifies wudu according to Hanafis.

In this lesson, we examine a key Hanafi proof for the nullifiers of wudu. The ruling rests on the principle that the Arabic word 'ma' indicates generality ('umum). This leads to a dispute with the Maliki school, which restricts nullification to habitual discharges. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of how Hanafi usul principles are applied to fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on nullifiers of wudu
What exactly does the Hanafi school rule regarding exits from the front and back passages? State the ruling clearly.
The ruling is that any exit, habitual or not, nullifies wudu.
2
Identify the Maliki view and its basis
What does Imam Malik hold? On what evidence or principle does he base his restriction?
Malik restricts to habitual exits based on custom ('urf).
3
Analyse the Maliki proof
Understand the reasoning behind the Maliki position. How does custom play a role in restricting the general wording?
Custom suggests that only usual discharges are considered in legal rulings.
4
Explain the Hanafi response using the generality of 'ma'
How do Hanafis counter the Maliki argument? What usul principle do they invoke?
The word 'ma' is a word of 'umum; it cannot be restricted by custom without textual evidence.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
What is the concrete difference in rulings between the two schools?
Malikis do not require wudu for non-habitual exits; Hanafis require wudu for any exit.

Source Text

(۷) إِنَّ كَلِمَةَ ”مَا عَامَّةٌ .
ترجمہ: کلمہ أو عموم کے لئے آتا ہے۔

Chapter on the Nullifiers of Wudu. (7) Indeed the word 'ma' is general. Translation: The word 'ma' is for generality. Explanation: The author of al-Hidayah has mentioned this principle to prove that everything that exits from the two passages (front and back) is a nullifier of wudu, whether it is habitual or non-habitual. Habitual like urine and feces, and non-habitual like a worm, pebble, and blood of istihadah. All these are nullifiers. According to Imam Malik, non-habitual things like worm, pebble, blood of istihadah, continuous urine, and incontinence do not nullify wudu. According to Hanafis, 'ma yakhruju min al-sabilayn' – everything that exits from the two passages nullifies wudu. Now the word 'ma' is general and absolute, including everything that exits from the two passages, whether habitual like urine and feces or non-habitual like a worm and pebble.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof that defines the nullifiers of wudu. The Hanafis use the linguistic principle that the word 'ma' (ما) indicates generality ('umum). In the principle 'everything that exits from the two passages nullifies wudu', the word 'ma' includes both habitual and non-habitual matters. The passage contrasts with Imam Malik, who limits nullifiers to habitual exits. The Hanafis argue that the generality of 'ma' covers all exiting matter, regardless of custom. Examples include urine, feces, worms, pebbles, and blood from istihadah.

Key Terms
نواقض الوضوء (nawaqid al-wudu): Things that invalidate wudu; here specifically exiting matter from the private parts.
ما (ma): The Arabic word for 'what' or 'that which'; used here as a relative pronoun to indicate generality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only urine and feces
B) Urine, feces, and blood, but not worms
C) Anything exiting from the front or back passage, including worms and pebbles
D) Only blood from istihadah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling that any exit from the private parts nullifies wudu is based on the linguistic generality of the word 'ma', overriding custom-based restrictions.

Tartib in Wudu: Sunnah Mu'akkadah, Not Fard
Ruling with proof on tartib in wudu.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tartib in wudu and explain the linguistic proof that supports it.

Core Idea
Order in wudu is sunnah, not fard, because the Arabic 'waw' does not indicate sequence.

In this lesson, we examine the legal status of washing the limbs in a specific order (tartib) during wudu. The Hanafi school holds that tartib is not obligatory but an emphasized sunnah. Let's explore the linguistic and hadith-based reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is performing the limbs of wudu (face, hands, head, feet) in the order mentioned in the Quran. The ruling is sunnah mu'akkadah, not fard or wajib.
2
Understand the linguistic argument about the waw
The conjunction 'waw' in the Quranic verse (e.g., fa-ghsilu wujuhakum wa aydiyakum) does not imply sequence. According to the majority of grammarians, as cited by Abu Ali al-Farisi and Sibawayh, the waw is for absolute conjunction (jam' mutlaq), not sequential order.
The Hanafi school relies on this linguistic consensus.
3
Evaluate the claim of consensus
The author defends the claim that there is consensus (ijma') among linguists that waw does not indicate order. Rare dissent does not break consensus.
4
Note the opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i considers tartib fard, based on the letter 'fa' in the verse (fa-ghsilu) which he interprets as indicating order. The Hanafi response is that the 'fa' applies to the succession of groups of limbs, not the sequence between individual limbs.
5
Apply the ruling in practice
If someone deliberately or forgetfully washes the arms before the face, their wudu is still valid (sahih) because tartib is not a condition. However, intentionally omitting the order without excuse is makruh tanzihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وَإِنَّمَا الْكَلَامُ فِي تَرْتِيبِ الْأَعْضَاءِ ، وَالدَّاخِلُ فِيهَا الْوَاوُ وَهِيَ لَا تُفِيدُ التَّرْتِيبَ، فَإِنْ قِيلَ: كَيْفَ ادَّعَى الْمُصَنِّفُ إِجْمَاعَ أَهْلِ اللُّغَةِ وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ يَقُولُ إِنَّهُ يُفِيدُ التَّرْتِيبَ وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ يَقُولُ يُفِيدُ الْقِرَانَ، أُجِيبَ بِأَنَّ أَبَا عَلَى الْفَارِسِيَّ ذَكَرَ أَنَّ التَّحَاةَ أَجْمَعُوا أَنَّ الْوَاوَ لِلْجَمْعِ الْمُطْلَقِ، ذَكَرَهُ سِيبَوَيْهِ فِى سَبْعَةَ عَشَرَ مَوْضِعًا فِي كِتَابِهِ فَاعْتَمَدَ الْمُصَنِّفُ عَلَى ذَلِكَ وَبِأَنَّ خِلَافَ الْقَلِيلِ لَا يَمْنَعُ الْإِجْمَاعَ اللُّغَوِيَّ.
العناية كتاب الطهارات ج ۱ ص ۳۵، ط: دار الفکر "الترتيب "سنة مؤكدة في الصحيح وهو كما نص الله تعالى في كتابه ولم يكن فرضا لأن الواو في الأمر لمطلق الجمع والفاء التى فى قوله تعالى (فَاغْسِلُوا) لتعقيب جملة الأعضاء .
مراقي الفلاح فصل فى سنن الوضوء، ص ۳۴، ط: المكتبة العصرية ]

Between them, order is not obligatory. But because in the blessed hadiths, mention of wudu is according to the Quranic order, therefore order in wudu is an emphatic sunnah. And the discussion is regarding the arrangement of the limbs, and the letter waw enters into it, and it does not indicate order. If it is said: How did the author claim consensus of the linguists, while among them are those who say that it indicates order and among them are those who say it indicates conjunction? It is answered: Abu Ali al-Farisi mentioned that the grammarians have agreed that the waw is for absolute conjunction; Sibawayh mentioned it in seventeen places in his book, so the author relied on that, and because the rare difference does not prevent linguistic consensus. Al-Inayah: Kitab al-Taharat, vol. 1, p. 35, Dar al-Fikr: "Order is an emphatic sunnah in the correct view, and it is as Allah stated in His Book, and it was not obligatory because the waw in the command is for absolute conjunction, and the fa' in His statement (so wash) is for the succession of the group of limbs." Muraqi al-Falah: Fasl fi Sunan al-Wudu, p. 34, al-Maktabah al-Asriyyah. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Taharat, vol. 1, p. 24, Rahmaniyyah.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the legal status of tartib (order) in wudu. The author states that order among the limbs is not fard (obligatory), but because the hadiths describe wudu in the Quranic order, it is sunnah mu'akkadah (emphasized sunnah). The proof is a linguistic analysis of the letter waw (و) in the Quranic verse for wudu. The waw is used for absolute conjunction (al-jam' al-mutlaq), not for sequential order (tartib). The author defends the claim of linguistic consensus by citing Abu Ali al-Farisi and Sibawayh. The rare disagreement of some grammarians does not negate the consensus. Thus, tartib is not a fard but a sunnah. In the Hanafi school, the mu'tamad position is that tartib is sunnah mu'akkadah, as mentioned in al-Inayah. The hadiths mentioning order make it recommended, but the lack of waw indicating order prevents it from being obligatory.

Key Terms
ترتیب (tartib): Order or sequence of washing the limbs in wudu according to the Quranic order.
الواو (al-waw): The Arabic letter waw used as a conjunction meaning 'and'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Sunnah mu'akkadah, because the waw does not indicate order
C) Wajib, because of the hadith
D) Mubah, optional

Key Takeaway
Tartib in wudu is sunnah mu'akkadah based on the linguistic consensus that the waw does not indicate order.

intermediate
6 min
Nullification of Wudu by Anything Exiting the Two Passages
Inter-madhhab dispute on nullifiers of wudu based on linguistic generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers any exit from the two passages a nullifier of wudu and evaluate the Maliki counter-argument.

Core Idea
The generality of the word 'ma' in 'ma yakhruju min al-sabilayn' includes all exits, even non-customary ones.

This lesson examines a classical dispute between the Hanafi and Maliki schools over what constitutes a nullifier of wudu. The Hanafi school, relying on the linguistic generality of the word 'ma' (what), holds that anything exiting from the front or rear passages nullifies wudu, whether customary or not. The Maliki school restricts nullification to customary excretions. Understanding this dispute clarifies how usul principles shape fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
What exactly is being contested? The Hanafi school says any exit from sabilayn nullifies wudu; the Maliki school says only customary exits (urine, feces, wind) nullify.
Non-customary exits include pebbles, insects, and istihadah blood.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
The Hanafi position is that everything exiting from the two passages nullifies wudu. The proof is the hadith 'ma yakhruju min al-sabilayn' where 'ma' is general and absolute, covering all exits without exception.
The word 'ma' is 'amm (general) in Arabic; Hanafis take it at face value.
3
State the opposing Maliki position and its proof
Imam Malik holds that only customary things (urine, feces, wind) nullify wudu. Non-customary things (pebble, insect, istihadah blood) do not. The proof is that the law is only concerned with usual states, and unusual exits are akin to things that exit from other parts of the body (like blood from a wound), which do not nullify wudu.
This is based on a principle of restricting general texts to common occurrences.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the word 'ma' is unrestricted in the hadith, and there is no evidence to restrict it to customary things. The consensus of linguists supports its generality. Also, other legal texts using 'ma' (e.g., in manumission and khul') are applied generally, proving consistency in Hanafi usul.
The author quotes examples from al-Jami' al-Kabir and al-Inayah to illustrate the generality of 'ma' in other contexts.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference: if a pebble exits from the anus during wudu, according to Hanafis, wudu is broken and must be renewed; according to Malikis, wudu remains valid.
This affects daily practice, especially for those with medical conditions like istihadah or chronic diarrhea.
