تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ نے اصول مذکورہ کو ذکر کر کے اس بات کو ثابت کیا ہے کہ ہر وہ چیز جو سبیلین سے خارج ہو وہ ناقض وضو ہے، خواہ وہ شی معتاد ہو یا غیر معتادشی ، معتاد جیسے بول و براز اور غیر معتاد جیسے کیڑا ، کنکر اور استحاضہ کا خون، یہ سب ناقض وضو ہیں ۔ امام مالک رحمہ اللہ کے ہاں غیر معتاد چیزیں مثلاً : کیڑا، کنکر، استحاضہ کا خون مسلسل البول، انطلاق بطن وغیرہ ناقض وضو نہیں ہے۔ احناف کے ہاں ما يَخْرُجُ مِنَ السَّبِيلَيْنِ “ کہ ہر وہ چیز جو سبیلین سے نکلے ناقض وضو ہے۔ اب اس میں کلمہ ” عام اور مطلق ہے، جو سبیلین سے نکلنے والی ہر چیز کو شامل ہوگا خواہ وہ معتاد ہو جیسے : بول و براز اور غیر معتاد جیسے : کیڑا،کنکر وغیرہ۔
لہذا کلمہ ”ما اپنی عمومیت کی وجہ سے سبیلین سے نکلنے والی ہر چیز کو شامل ہوگا اور اس سے وضو ٹوٹ جائے گا۔
قَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ فِي الْجَامِعِ الْكَبِيرِ : إِذَا قَالَ لِأَمَتِهِ إِنْ كَانَ مَا فِي بَطْنِكَ ذَكَرًا فَأَنْتِ حُرَّةٌ فَوَلَدَتْ غُلَامًا وَجَارِيَةً لَا تَعْتِقُ لِأَنَّ كَلِمَةَ "مَا عَامَّةٌ فَتَقْتَضِى أَنْ يَكُونَ جَمِيعُ مَا فِي بَطْنِهَا ذَكَرًا. الجوهرة النيرة : كتاب العتق، ج ٢ ص ١٠٣ ، ط: المطبعة الخيرية ]
(فَإِنْ قَالَتْ لَهُ خَالِعُنِي عَلَى مَا فِي يَدَيَّ فَخَالَعَهَا فَلَمْ يَكُنُ فِي يَدِهَا شَيْءٌ فَلَا شَيْءٍ لَهُ عَلَيْهَا لِأَنَّهَا لَمْ تَغُرَّهُ بِتَسْمِيَةِ الْمَالِ لِأَنَّ كَلِمَةَ "مَا عَامَّةٌ تَتَنَاوَلُ الْمَالَ وَغَيْرَهُ.
العناية: كتاب الطلاق، باب الخلع، ج ۴ ص ۲۲۲، ط: دار الفکر الهداية كتاب الطهارات، ج ۱ ص ۲۴، ط: رحمانیه
Chapter: On Nullifiers of Wudu. (7) Indeed, the word "ma" is general. Translation: The word is used for generality. Explanation: The author of al-Hidayah, by mentioning the aforementioned principle, has proved that everything that exits from the two passages is a nullifier of wudu, whether it is a customary thing or an unusual thing. Customary things like urine and feces, and unusual things like an insect, a pebble, and the blood of istihadah. All these are nullifiers of wudu. According to Imam Malik (may Allah have mercy on him), unusual things such as an insect, pebble, blood of istihadah, continuous urine, diarrhea, etc., are not nullifiers of wudu. According to the Hanafis, "what exits from the two passages" means everything that exits from the two passages is a nullifier of wudu. So in this, the word "ma" is general and absolute, including everything that exits from the two passages, whether customary like urine and feces, or unusual like an insect, pebble, etc. Therefore, the word "ma" due to its generality includes everything exiting from the two passages, and wudu is nullified by it. Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Kabir: If he says to his slave girl, "If what is in your womb is a male, then you are free," and she gives birth to a boy and a girl, she does not become free because the word "ma" is general, requiring that everything in her womb be male. (Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah: Kitab al-'Itq, vol. 2, p. 103, al-Matba'ah al-Khayriyyah). (If she says to him, "Divorce me in exchange for what is in my hand," and he divorces her, and there was nothing in her hand, then there is nothing for him from her because she did not deceive him by specifying wealth, since the word "ma" is general, covering wealth and other things. (Al-Inayah: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Khul', vol. 4, p. 222, Dar al-Fikr; al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Taharat, vol. 1, p. 24, Rahmaniyyah).
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the nullifiers of wudu (nawaqid al-wudu). The chapter heading introduces the topic. The seventh principle states that the word 'ma' (ما) is general ('amm). The author of al-Hidayah uses this linguistic principle to prove that everything exiting from the two passages (sabilayn)—the front and rear—nullifies wudu. This includes both customary substances like urine and feces, and non-customary ones like a worm, pebble, or the blood of istihadah (chronic uterine bleeding). The Hanafi position is that all such exits nullify wudu based on the generality of the word 'ma' in the hadith 'ma yakhruju min al-sabilayn' (what exits from the two passages). Imam Malik holds that only customary things nullify wudu, not unusual things. The Hanafi school relies on the absolute generality of 'ma'. To reinforce this, the author quotes from al-Jami' al-Kabir and al-Inayah showing that 'ma' is general in other legal contexts, such as manumission and khul'. Thus, the Hanafi position is that any exit from the two passages, regardless of its nature, nullifies wudu. The mufta bihi position is the Hanafi view.

Key Terms
ما (ma): The Arabic word meaning 'what', used as a general particle indicating all-inclusiveness.
سبیلین (sabilayn): The two passages: the front (urethra/vagina) and the rear (anus).
ناقض وضو (naqid al-wudu): An act or occurrence that invalidates wudu, requiring renewal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, does the exit of a small pebble from the anus nullify wudu?
A) No, because it is not a customary excretion
B) Yes, because the word 'ma' is general and includes all exits
C) Yes, but only if it is wet
D) No, unless it causes one to pass wind

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that any exit from the two passages, customary or not, nullifies wudu due to the unrestricted generality of the word 'ma' in the prophetic hadith.

Nullification by Exit of Najasah from Non-Sabilayn with Flow
Explains that impurities exiting other than the two passages also nullify wudu if they flow

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on nullification of wudu by impurities from non-sabilayn and apply the condition of flow using qiyas.

Core Idea
Impurities from non-sabilayn nullify wudu only if they flow—qiyas on the two passages.

Is every drop of blood from a wound a wudu-breaker? The Hanafi school says no—it must flow. This lesson unpacks the rational proof (dalil 'aqli) from al-Hidayah, which uses qiyas to extend the ruling from the two passages to other exits, adding a critical condition: flow (sayalan). Understanding this distinction prevents incorrect assumptions about wudu validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Asl (Original Case)
Start with the agreed-upon case: impurity exiting from the two passages (sabilayn) definitely nullifies wudu. This is the asl.
This is based on hadith and ijma.
2
Identify the Far' (New Case)
The new case is impurity exiting from other parts of the body, such as blood or pus from a wound. Does it also nullify wudu?
3
Determine the Illa (Effective Cause)
The Hanafi analysis identifies the illa as the exit of impurity that is flowing and reaches a place that must be purified in wudu/ghusl. Both the asl and far' share this.
The illa is extracted via tahqiq al-manat.
4
Apply the Condition of Flow
For the far', the ruling is that wudu is nullified only if the impurity flows. Mere appearance is insufficient. This condition is deduced from the hadith about vomit and nosebleed (which exit from non-sabilayn) and rational extension.
Flow is defined as the impurity moving beyond the point of exit to an area that would need washing.
5
Recognize Cross-Madhhab Dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that only exit from sabilayn nullifies wudu. The Hanafi response is that the hadith itself includes vomit and nosebleed as nullifiers, establishing an analogy.
The practical consequence: a minor cut with no flow does not break wudu in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۸) إِنَّ خُرُوجَ النَّجَاسَةِ مُؤَثِرٌ فِي زَوَالِ الطَّهَارَةِ.
ترجمہ: نجاست کا نکلنا طہارت کے زائل ہونے میں مؤثر ہے۔
تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ نے اصول مذکورہ کو بطور دلیل عقلی کے اس بات کو ثابت کرنے کے لئے پیش ہے کہ خارج من غیر السبیلین میں سے بھی بعض ایسی چیزیں ہیں جو ناقض وضو ہیں اور ان کے نکلنے سے وضوٹوٹ جاتا ہے جیسے خون، پیپ وغیرہ ،البتہ اس میں شرط یہ ہے کہ خون، پیپ وغیرہ جسم سے نکل کر بہہ جائے اور ایسے مقام کی طرف تجاوز کر جائے جس کو غسل یا وضو میں پاک کرنے کا حکم ہے یعنی سبیلین کے علاوہ دوسرے مقام سے خروج میں صرف نجاست کا ظاہر ہونا کافی نہیں ہے بلکہ سیلان شرط ہے۔ بہر حال اصول مذکورہ اور دلیل کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ اتنی بات تو مسلم ہے کہ سبیلین سے خروج نجاست کی صورت میں طہارت زائل ہو جاتی ہے، یہ صورت اصل اور منفیس علیہ ہے اور حدیث میں قی اور رعاف وغیرہ کو ناقض وضو شمار کیا گیا ہے اور یہ چیزیں غیر سبیلین سے خارج ہوتی ہیں ، تو اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ خارج من السبیلین بھی ناقض وضو ہے اور خارج من غیر السبیلین بھی ناقض وضو ہے اور دونوں صورتوں میں انسان کی طہارت زائل ہو جاتی ہے اور زوال طہارت کے بعد حصول طہارت کے لئے اعضاء اربعہ کو لازماً دھونا پڑتا ہے اور چونکہ اصل یعنی خارج من اسبیلین
(8) Indeed, the exit of impurity is effective in the removal of purity. Translation: The exit of impurity has an effect in removing purity. Explanation: The author of al-Hidayah presents the aforementioned principle as a rational proof to prove that among things exiting from other than the two passages, some things are nullifiers of wudu and their exit breaks wudu, such as blood, pus, etc. However, the condition in this is that blood, pus, etc., exit from the body and flow, and pass over to a place that is commanded to be purified in ghusl or wudu. That is, in the case of exit from a place other than the two passages, merely the appearance of impurity is not sufficient; rather, flowing is a condition. In any case, the outcome of the mentioned principle and proof is that it is agreed that in the case of exit of impurity from the two passages, purity is removed; this is the original case and the analogized case. And in the hadith, vomiting and nosebleed etc. are counted as nullifiers of wudu, and these things exit from other than the two passages. So from this it is known that exit from the two passages is also a nullifier of wudu, and exit from other than the two passages is also a nullifier of wudu. In both cases, a person's purity is removed, and after the removal of purity, to obtain purity, it is necessary to wash the four limbs. And since the original case is the exit from the two passages...
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with a rational proof (dalil 'aqli) regarding the nullification of wudu by impurities exiting from places other than the two passages. The eighth principle states that the exit of najasah (impurity) is effective in removing purity. The author uses this as a basis for analogy (qiyas). The established case (asl) is that impurity exiting from the two passages nullifies wudu. The new case (far') is impurity exiting from other parts of the body, such as blood from a wound or pus. The Hanafi school requires that for such impurities be nullifiers, they must flow (sayalan) from the body and reach a point that would need washing in wudu or ghusl. Mere appearance is not enough. This is supported by hadith about vomit and nosebleed being nullifiers. Thus, both exits from the two passages and exits from other passages (with flow) nullify wudu. The mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school is that blood, pus, etc., that flow from a wound nullify wudu, but if they do not flow, they do not. This lesson demonstrates the use of qiyas in deriving fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
نجاست (najasah): Impurity, such as blood, pus, urine, feces, etc.
سیلان (sayalan): Flowing: the condition that the impurity must flow from the body to nullify wudu.
طہارت (taharah): Purity, specifically wudu or ghusl in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does blood from a wound nullify wudu?
A) Always, whenever any blood appears
B) Only if it flows from the wound
C) Never, blood does not nullify wudu
D) Only if it is from the two passages

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, impurities from non-sabilayn only nullify wudu if they flow; this is derived via qiyas from the two passages and supported by hadith.

The Generality of the Word 'Ma' in Hanafi Fiqh
Establishes the principle of generality of the word 'ma'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal principle that the word 'ma' is general and apply it to examples in Taharah, manumission, and khul'.

Core Idea
The word 'ma' is general—it includes everything it refers to, unless specified otherwise.

This passage from Al-Hidayah establishes a key linguistic principle in Hanafi fiqh: the word 'ma' (ما) operates as a general term ('amm) that covers all possible referents. This has direct implications for rulings such as the nullification of wudu when anything exits from the two passages. Additionally, the principle is applied in other areas like manumission and khul' (divorce by redemption). Understanding this generality is essential for correct legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its legal classification
Recognize that the word 'ma' is being treated as a general term ('amm) that includes all its possible instances. In Hanafi usul, 'amm is a category of text that indicates absolute generality until specified otherwise.
The principle is stated in the opening sentence: 'لہذا کلمہ "ما" اپنی عمومیت کی وجہ سے...'
2
Understand the primary ruling on wudu
The passage directly applies the generality of 'ma' to the nullification of wudu: any substance exiting from the two passages (sebilayn) breaks wudu because the word 'ma' covers all such exits.
This is consistent with the well-known Hanafi ruling that any discharge from the front or rear invalidates wudu.
3
Analyze the first expansion example (manumission)
In the case from al-Jami' al-Kabir, a master says to his slave: 'If what is in your womb is a male, you are free.' She delivers twins (a boy and a girl). The condition is not fulfilled because 'ma' requires that everything in the womb be male. The generality of 'ma' prevents a partial fulfillment.
Al-Jawhara al-Nayyirah is cited as the source for this example.
4
Analyze the second expansion example (khul')
In khul', a wife says: 'Divorce me in exchange for what is in my hand.' If nothing is in her hand, nothing is owed because 'ma' includes both property and non-property. No deception occurred because no specific amount was mentioned.
Al-'Inayah is cited; the ruling shows that 'ma' covers all possibilities, protecting against claims of fraud.
5
Synthesize the principle
The generality of 'ma' is consistent across different chapters of fiqh—Taharah, manumission, and divorce. This demonstrates the Hanafi commitment to linguistic literalness and the importance of usul al-fiqh in deriving consistent rulings.
The principle applies unless a specific exclusion (takhsis) is proven.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لہذا کلمہ ”ما“ اپنی عمومیت کی وجہ سے سبیلین سے نکلنے والی ہر چیز کو شامل ہوگا اور اس سے وضو ٹوٹ جائے گا۔ قَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ فِي الْجَامِعِ الْكَبِيرِ : إِذَا قَالَ لِأَمَتِهِ إِنْ كَانَ مَا فِي بَطْنِكَ ذَكَرًا فَأَنْتِ حُرَّةٌ فَوَلَدَتْ غُلَامًا وَجَارِيَةً لَا تَعْتِقُ لِأَنَّ كَلِمَةَ "مَا عَامَّةٌ فَتَقْتَضِى أَنْ يَكُونَ جَمِيعُ مَا فِي بَطْنِهَا ذَكَرًا. الجوهرة النيرة : كتاب العتق، ج ٢ ص ١٠٣ ، ط: المطبعة الخيرية ] فَإِنْ قَالَتْ لَهُ خَالِعُنِي عَلَى مَا فِي يَدَيَّ فَخَالَعَهَا فَلَمْ يَكُنُ فِي يَدِهَا شَيْءٌ فَلَا شَيْءٍ لَهُ عَلَيْهَا لِأَنَّهَا لَمْ تَغُرَّهُ بِتَسْمِيَةِ الْمَالِ لِأَنَّ كَلِمَةَ "مَا عَامَّةٌ تَتَنَاوَلُ الْمَالَ وَغَيْرَهُ. العناية: كتاب الطلاق، باب الخلع، ج ۴ ص ۲۲۲، ط: دار الفکر الهداية كتاب الطهارات، ج ۱ ص ۲۴، ط: رحمانیه
Therefore, the word 'ma' due to its generality will include everything exiting from the two passages, and wudu will break from it. Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Kabir: When a man says to his female slave, 'If what is in your womb is a male, then you are free,' and she gives birth to a boy and a girl, she is not freed because the word 'ma' is general and requires that all that is in her womb be a male. (al-Jawhara al-Nayyirah: Book of Manumission, vol. 2, p. 103) And if she says to him, 'Divorce me in exchange for what is in my hand,' and he divorces her, and nothing is in her hand, then nothing is owed to him from her because she did not deceive him by specifying property, since the word 'ma' is general, covering property and other things. (al-'Inayah: Book of Divorce, Chapter of Khul', vol. 4, p. 222)
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage introduces a principle concerning the word 'ma' (ما). The commentary explains that 'ma' is a general term that includes everything. Therefore, when a substance exits from the two passages (sebilayn), the word 'ma' in the general ruling of nullification of wudu covers all such exits, so wudu breaks. Two examples from Hanafi texts demonstrate this: (1) In a conditional manumission, if a master says to his slave, 'If what is in your womb is a male, you are free,' and she delivers twins (a boy and a girl), the condition is not fulfilled because 'ma' requires everything in the womb to be male. (2) In khul', if a wife says, 'Divorce me for what is in my hand,' and she has nothing, nothing is owed because 'ma' includes property and non-property, thus no deception occurred. These examples show that 'ma' retains its generality even in legal contexts.

Key Terms
ما (ma): A general word that includes all possible referents, used in this passage to mean 'whatever' or 'that which'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle stated in this passage, what happens to wudu when something exits from the two passages (sebilayn)?
A) Wudu is nullified.
B) Wudu is not affected.
C) Only wudu from previous prayer is nullified.
D) It depends on the substance.

Key Takeaway
The word 'ma' is general in Hanafi fiqh; it includes everything it refers to, and this principle is applied consistently across different rulings.

Effective Cause: Exit of Impurity Removes Purity
States a rational principle for nullification of wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi proof that flowing impurities from anywhere on the body break wudu, using the principle of 'khuruj al-najasah mu'aththir' and the required condition of flow (saylan).

Core Idea
Any flowing impurity removes purity, regardless of the exit point.

We know that exiting urine or stool breaks wudu. But what about blood, pus, or vomit? The great Hanafi jurist al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah presents a rational principle that the exit of impurity itself is the effective cause for removing purity. This lesson unpacks that principle and the qiyas jaliy that extends the ruling from the two passages to all other body parts, with a vital condition: the impurity must flow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
State the principle: 'The exit of impurity is effective in the removal of purity.' Understand that this is a rational (aqli) claim based on the nature of impurity.
The principle is not derived from a single text but from the common legal meaning across sources.
2
Recognize the agreed case (asl)
Identify that exit of impurity from the two passages (sebilayn) is universally agreed to break wudu. This forms the original case for analogy.
Ijma and hadith support this.
3
Analyze the hadith evidence for other exits
The source cites that hadith mentions vomiting and nosebleed as nullifiers. These exit from other than the two passages, showing that impurity exits elsewhere also breaks wudu.
This confirms that the effective cause is the impurity itself, not the location.
4
Extract the illa and apply qiyas jaliy
The common element (illa) is 'khuruj najasah mu'aththir fi zawal al-taharah' (exit of impurity that removes purity). Since this illa is present in both asl (sebilayn) and far' (non-sebilayn), the ruling transfers: both nullify wudu and require washing the four limbs.
This is qiyas jaliy (obvious analogy) because the illa is explicit and clear.
5
Note the condition of flow (saylan)
For non-sebilayn impurities, merely appearing is not enough; the impurity must flow to a place that is ruled to be purified in wudu or ghusl. This ensures that the impurity actually spreads and requires washing.
Flow is a condition (shart) for nullification from non-sebilayn. Other schools may not require flow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ خُرُوجَ النَّجَاسَةِ مُؤَثِرٌ فِي زَوَالِ الطَّهَارَةِ. ترجمہ: نجاست کا نکلنا طہارت کے زائل ہونے میں مؤثر ہے۔ تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ نے اصول مذکورہ کو بطور دلیل عقلی کے اس بات کو ثابت کرنے کے لئے پیش ہے کہ خارج من غیر السبیلین میں سے بھی بعض ایسی چیزیں ہیں جو ناقض وضو ہیں اور ان کے نکلنے سے وضوٹوٹ جاتا ہے جیسے خون، پیپ وغیرہ ،البتہ اس میں شرط یہ ہے کہ خون، پیپ وغیرہ جسم سے نکل کر بہہ جائے اور ایسے مقام کی طرف تجاوز کر جائے جس کو غسل یا وضو میں پاک کرنے کا حکم ہے یعنی سبیلین کے علاوہ دوسرے مقام سے خروج میں صرف نجاست کا ظاہر ہونا کافی نہیں ہے بلکہ سیلان شرط ہے۔ بہر حال اصول مذکورہ اور دلیل کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ اتنی بات تو مسلم ہے کہ سبیلین سے خروج نجاست کی صورت میں طہارت زائل ہو جاتی ہے، یہ صورت اصل اور منفیس علیہ ہے اور حدیث میں قی اور رعاف وغیرہ کو ناقض وضو شمار کیا گیا ہے اور یہ چیزیں غیر سبیلین سے خارج ہوتی ہیں ، تو اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ خارج من السبیلین بھی ناقض وضو ہے اور خارج من غیر السبیلین بھی ناقض وضو ہے اور دونوں صورتوں میں انسان کی طہارت زائل ہو جاتی ہے اور زوال طہارت کے بعد حصول طہارت کے لئے اعضاء اربعہ کو لازماً دھونا پڑتا ہے اور چونکہ اصل یعنی خارج من اسبیلین میں حصول طہارت کے لئے اعضاء اربعہ کو دھویا جاتا ہے تو اسی پر قیاس کر کے فرع یعنی خارج من غیر السبیلین میں بھی اعضاء اربعہ کے دھونے کا حکم لگایا جائے گا، کیونکہ دونوں صورتوں میں انسان کی طہارت زائل ہوتی ہے، اسی لئے صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا کہ نجاست کا نکلنا طہارت کے زائل ہونے میں مؤثر ہے اور نجاست کا خروج چونکہ دونوں صورتوں میں ہوتا ہے لہذا نسل میں دونوں کا حکم ایک ہوگا۔
Indeed, the exit of impurity is effective in the removal of purity. Translation: The exit of impurity is effective in the removal of purity. Commentary: The author of Hidayah presents this principle as a rational proof to establish that some things exiting from other than the two passages are nullifiers of wudu, and upon their exit wudu breaks, such as blood, pus, etc. However, the condition in this is that blood, pus, etc. exit from the body and flow, and exceed to a place that is ruled to be purified in ghusl or wudu; i.e., in exiting from a place other than the two passages, merely the appearance of impurity is not sufficient, rather flow is a condition. In any case, the summary of this principle and proof is that it is agreed that in the case of exit of impurity from the two passages, purity is removed; this is the original (asl) and the base case (manfis alayh). In hadith, vomiting and nosebleed etc. are counted as nullifiers of wudu, and these exit from other than the two passages. So it follows that exit from the two passages is also a nullifier, and exit from other than the two passages is also a nullifier. In both cases, a person's purity is removed. After the removal of purity, to attain purity, it is necessary to wash the four limbs. Since in the original (exit from the two passages) the four limbs are washed for attaining purity, by analogy the branch (exit from other than the two passages) is also ruled to wash the four limbs, because in both cases purity is removed. Hence the author of Hidayah said that the exit of impurity is effective in removing purity, and since impurity exits in both cases, therefore the ruling of both is the same.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This is a ruling with a rational proof (dalil aqli). The text states the principle that the exit of impurity causes the removal of ritual purity (taharah). The author of Hidayah uses this as evidence that impurities exiting from places other than the two passages (non-sebilayn) also nullify wudu, provided they flow (saylan). The proof is that it is agreed that exit from the two passages nullifies wudu (asal). Hadith mentions that vomiting and nosebleeds, which exit from other places, are also nullifiers. This indicates that the effective cause ('illa) is the exit of impurity itself, regardless of location. Therefore, a qiyas jaliy (obvious analogy) is made: just as wudu is broken by the exit of impurity from the two passages and requires washing the four limbs, similarly wudu breaks from non-sebilayn impurities and requires the same. The condition of flow is added to ensure that the impurity reaches a place that must be purified.

Key Terms
نجاسة (najasah): Impurity that exits the body, e.g., blood, pus, urine, feces
طہارت (taharah): Ritual purity that can be removed by the exit of impurity
سیلان (saylan): Flow of impurity to a point that requires washing in ritual purification

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle presented, what makes the exit of impurity from other than the two passages break wudu?
A) The impurity must be from the head or nose.
B) The impurity must be foul-smelling.
C) The impurity must flow to a place that must be purified.
D) The impurity must be from food or drink.

Key Takeaway
The exit of impurity is the effective cause for removing purity, but for non-sebilayn impurities, flow is a necessary condition for nullification of wudu.

The Principle of Stickiness and Phlegm Vomit
States a rational principle and applies it to phlegm vomit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle that stickiness prevents impurity penetration and apply it to determine when vomited matter nullifies wudu.

Core Idea
Stickiness blocks impurity penetration; phlegm vomit does not break wudu.

This lesson introduces a rational principle from al-Hidayah: sticky substances do not allow impurity to penetrate. The author uses it to prove that phlegm (balgham) vomit is not a nullifier of wudu, distinguishing it from watery vomit. This is an example of istihsan based on physical properties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states that phlegm vomit does not nullify wudu. Recognise this is the legal conclusion.
2
Locate the proof
The proof is a rational principle (dalil aqli): stickiness prevents impurity penetration. Find it in the Arabic: 'لَا تَتَخَلَّلُهُ النَّجَاسَةُ'.
3
Apply the principle to the case
Phlegm is sticky (lazij), so impurity cannot enter it. Therefore, even if phlegm contains impurity, it remains pure and does not break wudu.
Contrast with watery vomit which is not sticky and allows impurity to spread.
4
Understand the basis
This is istihsan: preference based on a tangible quality. The principle is derived from observation of physical properties.
Refer to al-Binayah and al-Hidayah for context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الشَّيْءَ إِذَا كَانَ لَزِجْ لَا تَتَخَلَّلُهُ النَّجَاسَةُ. ترجمہ: ہر وہ چیز جس میں چکنا ہٹ ہو اس میں نجاست داخل نہیں ہوتی۔ تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ نے اصول مذکورہ کو بطور دلیل عقلی کے ذکر کر کے حضرات طرفین کے مسلک کو ثابت کیا ہے کہ بلغم کی قئی غیر ناقص وضو ہے۔ البناية: كتاب الطهارات, فصل فى نواقض الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۲۶۷، ط: دار الكتب العلمية الهداية: كتاب الطهارات، ج ۱ ص ۲۵، ط: رحمانیه
Indeed, when something is sticky, impurity does not penetrate it. Translation: Any substance that is sticky, impurity does not enter into it. Commentary: The author of Hidayah mentions this principle as a rational proof to establish the position of both parties (the scholars) that phlegm vomit is not a nullifier of wudu. al-Binayah: Book of Purification, Chapter on Nullifiers of Wudu, vol. 1, p. 267. al-Hidayah: Book of Purification, vol. 1, p. 25.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents a rational principle (dalil aqli) used to prove that phlegm (balgham) vomit does not break wudu. The principle states that sticky substances do not allow impurity to penetrate (takhallul). Therefore, even if phlegm contains traces of impurity, its sticky nature prevents the impurity from spreading, so it remains pure (tahir) and does not nullify wudu. This is cited from al-Binayah and al-Hidayah. The principle is specifically applied to phlegm, which is a sticky substance, as opposed to watery fluids that may carry impurity. This is an example of istihsan (juristic preference) based on a physical property.

Key Terms
لزج (lazij): Sticky, viscous; a quality that prevents impurity from penetrating
بلغم (balgham): Phlegm, a sticky mucous substance from the throat

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this principle, why does vomited phlegm not break wudu?
A) Because it is not impure.
B) Because it is sticky and impurity does not penetrate it.
C) Because it comes from the stomach.
D) Because it is a solid.

Key Takeaway
The stickiness of phlegm prevents impurity penetration, so vomiting phlegm does not nullify wudu.

Principle: Whatever is not a hadath is not najis
States a legal principle and applies to small vomit and non-flowing blood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle that whatever is not a hadath is not najis and apply it to determine the purity of small vomit and non-flowing blood.

Core Idea
A substance that does not break wudu cannot be physically impure.

This lesson introduces a foundational legal maxim from the Hanafi school: if an exiting substance does not nullify wudu (i.e., is not a hadath), it is also not physically impure (najis). This principle, narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf, clarifies the purity status of common bodily excretions like small amounts of vomit and non-flowing blood, which are often misunderstood to be impurities. Understanding this maxim is essential for correct practice in daily acts of worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal principle
The core maxim is: 'إن ما لا يكون حدثا لا يكون نجسا' – 'Whatever is not a hadath is not najis.' This is a qāʿidah fiqhiyyah that links ritual impurity (hadath) with physical impurity (najas).
The principle is stated in Arabic at the start of the passage.
2
Understand the logical link
The reasoning is that if a substance were truly najis, its exit from the body would necessarily break wudu (hadath). Since small vomit and non-flowing blood do not break wudu, they cannot be najis. This is a contrapositive inference.
The text uses 'ألا ترى' (do you not see) to highlight this logical connection.
3
Apply to specific cases
The principle is applied to 'قليل القيء' (small vomit) and 'غير السائل من الدم' (non-flowing blood). Both are ruled to be pure (tāhir) and do not nullify wudu.
These are the two examples explicitly mentioned in the source.
4
Trace the authority
The principle is narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf and is recorded in the zahir al-riwāyah texts, specifically in al-Hidayah and al-Binayah. Al-Marghinani adopts this view as the relied-upon position.
This establishes the authority weight as zahir al-riwāyah.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Imam al-Shafiʿi considers all vomit, even small amounts, as najis and nullifying wudu. The Hanafi school differs based on this principle. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf) is a difference in rulings on purity and wudu validity.
This ikhtilāf is acknowledged as legitimate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ مَا لَا يَكُونُ حَدَثَاً لَا يَكُونُ نَجَسًا.
ترجمہ: ہر وہ چیز جو حدث نہ ہو تو وہ پنجس بھی نہیں ہوگی۔
تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ نے ایک فقہی ضابطہ ذکر کیا ہے جو امام ابو یوسف رحمہ اللہ سے مروی ہے، جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ ہر وہ چیز جو حدث یعنی ناقض وضو نہ ہو تو وہ پنج یعنی نا پاک بھی نہیں ہوگی، جیسے قی قلیل اور دم غیر سائل وغیرہ یہ نجس نہیں ہیں، اگر یہ نجس ہوتیں تو ان کے نکلنے سے وضو ٹوٹ جاتا، حالانکہ معمولی قی اور دم غیر سائل وغیرہ سے وضو نہیں ٹوٹتا، جب وضو نہیں ٹوٹتا تو نجس بھی نہیں ہوگا۔
البناية: كتاب الطهارات، فصل فى نواقض الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۲۶۷، ط: دار الكتب العلمية الهداية: كتاب الطهارات، ج ۱ ص ۲۵، ط: رحمانیه
إن ما لا يكون حدثا لا يكون نجسا الذي لا يكون حدثا هو القليل من القيء وغير السائل من الدم لا يكون نجسا ألا ترى أنه لا تنقض به الطهارة فيكون طاهرا
[البناية: كتاب الطهارات، فصل فى نواقض الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۲۷۵، ط: دار الكتب العلمية وإنما اتصل به قليل القيء فلا يكون حدثا فلا يكون نجسا وكذا الصبي إذا ارتضع وقاء من ساعته. البحر الرائق: كتاب الطهارات، فصل فى نواقض الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۳۶، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي] الهداية كتاب الطهارات، ج ۱ ص ۲۶، ط: رحمانیه
Indeed, whatever is not a hadath is not najis. Translation: Everything that is not a hadath (i.e., does not break wudu) will also not be najis (impure). Explanation: The author of al-Hidayah mentioned a legal maxim narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, whose summary is that every thing that is not a hadath (i.e., a nullifier of wudu) will also not be najis (unclean), such as a small amount of vomit and non-flowing blood, etc. These are not najis. If they were najis, their exiting would break wudu, whereas small vomit and non-flowing blood do not break wudu. Since wudu is not broken, they are not najis. Al-Binayah: ... [source references]
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental legal principle (qāʿidah fiqhiyyah) narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf. It establishes a causal link between the concept of hadath (ritual impurity that nullifies wudu) and najāsah (physical impurity). The logic is: if a substance were truly najis, its exit would inevitably break wudu, because najis substances exiting from the body are generally considered hadath (e.g., urine, stool). However, since small amounts of vomit (qayʾ qalīl) and non-flowing blood (dam ghayr sāʾil) do not break wudu, they cannot be considered najis. The passage then cites the text of al-Hidayah and al-Binayah to confirm that small vomit and non-flowing blood are pure (tāhir). This principle is used to resolve cases where doubt arises about the purity of bodily exits. The key takeaway: the ruling on physical impurity depends on the ruling on ritual impurity for these substances.

Key Terms
حَدَث (ḥadath): A state of ritual impurity that nullifies wudu, such as passing wind, urine, or stool.
نَجَس (najas): Physical impurity (filth) that prevents prayer unless removed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle 'Whatever is not a hadath is not najis', which of the following is true?
A) Large vomit breaks wudu but is not najis.
B) Small, non-flowing blood from a wound is najis.
C) Small vomit does not break wudu and is not najis.
D) All vomit breaks wudu regardless of quantity.

Key Takeaway
Small vomit and non-flowing blood are pure and do not nullify wudu, based on the principle that whatever is not a hadath is not najis.

The Stomach Is Not a Place for Blood: Vomiting Blood and Wudu
Rational proof (talil) using qiyas to external wound blood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on vomiting blood as a nullifier of wudu and explain the qiyas linking it to external wound blood.

Core Idea
Blood vomited from the stomach breaks wudu if it flows, because it originates from a wound, not from digestion.

A common question in taharah is whether vomiting blood invalidates wudu. The Hanafi school gives a nuanced answer based on a foundational principle: the stomach is not a natural container for blood. This lesson unpacks the rational proof (talil) from al-Hidayah, showing how the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) analogize stomach blood to external wound blood, and explains the intra-madhhab disagreement with Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What act is being ruled upon? Vomiting blood. What is the ruling? It is a nullifier (naqid) of wudu according to the two shaykhs, provided the blood flows.
The flow condition (sail) is key; mere staining does not break wudu.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a combination of a rational principle ('the stomach is not a place for blood') and a qiyas: blood from the stomach is analogized to blood from an external wound, which is established as a nullifier when it flows.
The principle itself is a rational certainty (qat'i 'aqli) that the stomach is for food, not blood.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the principle prove the ruling? Since the stomach is not a place for blood, any blood found there must have come from a wound. Blood from a wound, if it flows, breaks wudu. Therefore, vomiting blood that flows also breaks wudu.
This is direct talil (causation) via qiyas.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that vomiting—even blood—does not break wudu unless the vomit fills the mouth. He considers vomiting a non-nullifying event in itself.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi; the mufta bihi is the view of the two shaykhs.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Later Hanafi authorities (e.g., al-Marghinani) side with the two shaykhs because the qiyas is strong and the principle is sound.
The relied-upon position is that flowing blood from vomiting breaks wudu, regardless of quantity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الْمِعْدَةَ لَيْسَتْ بِمَحَلَّ الدَّمِ.
ترجمہ: معدہ خون کی جگہ نہیں ہے۔
تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ نے اصول مذکورہ کو دلیل عقلی کے پیش نظر ذکر کر کے حضرات شیخین کا مسلک ثابت کیا ہے کہ اگر خون اپنی قوت کی وجہ سے بہہ جائے تو وہ ناقض وضو ہے۔ حاصل یہ ہے کہ حضرات شیخین کے نزدیک اگر خون اپنی قوت سے اپنی جگہ سے بہہ جائے اگر چہ وہ خون قلیل مقدار میں ہی کیوں نہ ہو تو اس سے وضو ٹوٹ جائے گا، اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ معدہ خون کی جگہ نہیں ہے، جب معدہ محل دم نہیں تو لا محالہ یہی کہا جائے گا کہ یہ نکلنے والا خون پیٹ کے کسی زخم سے نکلا ہے، پس اس کو اس خون پر قیاس کیا جائے گا جو ظاہر زخم سے نکلے، چونکہ اس میں
(لا تتخلله النجاسة أى لا يتداخله النجاسة ولا يدخل فى أجزائه وما يتصل به قليل والقليل في القيء غير ناقض لأنه لا يحتمل السيلان والسيلان في غير السبيلين أقيم مقام الخروج ولم يوجده.
[البناية: كتاب الطهارة، فصل فى نواقض الوضوء، ج ا ص ۲۷۶، ط: دار الكتب العلمية ]
Indeed, the stomach is not a container for blood. Translation: The stomach is not a place for blood. Explanation: The author of al-Hidayah mentioned this principle as a rational proof to establish the position of the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) that if blood flows due to its force, it is a nullifier of wudu. The gist is that according to the two shaykhs, if blood flows from its place due to its force, even if it is small in quantity, it breaks wudu. The reason is that the stomach is not a place for blood. Since the stomach is not a place for blood, inevitably it must be said that this exiting blood came from a wound in the stomach, so it will be analogized to blood that exits from an external wound. Because [it] does not allow penetration of impurity, meaning impurity does not interpenetrate it and does not enter its parts, and what is attached to it is small, and small [amount] in vomit is not a nullifier because it cannot flow, and flow in other than the two passages (front and back) is considered like exiting, and he did not find it. [al-Binayah...]
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents a third principle used as a rational proof to establish the position of the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) regarding blood that emerges through vomiting. The principle is that the stomach is not a natural container for blood. Therefore, if blood is vomited, it must have come from a wound in the stomach or digestive tract. This blood is then analogous to blood that flows from an external wound: if it flows (i.e., is not just a spot but flows by its own force), it breaks wudu, even if the quantity is small. The text also references the principle from al-Binayah that impurity does not penetrate a viscous substance, but that is a different principle. Here, the focus is on the origin of the blood. The argument is: since the stomach is not a place for blood, the presence of blood indicates a wound; blood from a wound, if flowing, breaks wudu. The key takeaway: vomiting blood that flows breaks wudu because it is considered like blood from an external wound.

Key Terms
مِعْدَة (miʿdah): Stomach; the organ of digestion.
سَيَلَان (sayalān): Flowing; movement of blood from its source due to force.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle 'The stomach is not a place for blood', when does vomiting blood break wudu?
A) When the blood is black and thick.
B) When the blood flows (is not static) regardless of quantity.
C) Only if the amount is large (fills the mouth).
D) Never, because vomit never breaks wudu.

Key Takeaway
Flowing blood from vomiting breaks wudu because the stomach is not a container for blood, so the blood must come from a wound and is analogized to external wound blood.

Why Phlegm in Vomiting Does Not Nullify Wudu
Explains why phlegm excreted in vomiting does not nullify wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on vomiting phlegm and explain the two-part proof based on the physical property of phlegm and the principle of minimal impurity.

Core Idea
Phlegm is smooth: impurity cannot penetrate it, and any impurity attached is minimal and excused.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah addresses a common question: does vomiting phlegm break wudu? The Hanafi school rules that it does not, based on a two-fold reasoning. First, phlegm is a thick, smooth substance that prevents the penetration of impurity. Second, any impurity that may accompany the phlegm is minimal, and a small amount of impurity in vomit is excused by the principle of 'afw. We will unpack this rationale and understand the Hanafi method of deriving rulings from physical properties and legal maxims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What specific action is being discussed? What is its ruling regarding wudu? Identify that vomiting phlegm does not break wudu.
2
Locate the first proof
What is the first reason given? Phlegm is smooth (chikna) and sticky, and impurity does not penetrate into such substances.
The Arabic phrase: 'لَا تَتَخَلَّلُهُ النَّجَاسَةُ' (impurity does not penetrate it).
3
Locate the second proof
What is the second reason? Any impurity that may be attached to the phlegm is minimal, and a small amount of impurity in vomit is excused (qalīl fī al-qay' ghayr nāqiḍ).
This relies on the legal principle that minimal impurity is pardoned in cases of vomiting.
4
Understand the underlying principle
How do these two proofs combine? The physical property prevents major impurity, and the legal principle handles the minor impurity that might escape.
This is an application of istihsan (juristic preference) based on custom and ease.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Do other schools agree? Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) hold that any vomiting of a mouthful, regardless of substance, breaks wudu. The Hanafi view is specific to thick substances and minimal impurity.
The text mentions 'both parties' indicating agreement between Abu Hanifa and his students, but not necessarily other madhhabs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلیل کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ حضرات طرفین کے نزدیک اگر کسی شخص نے بلغم کی قی کی تو اس سے وضو نہیں ٹوٹے گا، اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ بلغم میں چکناہٹ ہوتی ہے، اور اصول ہے کہ چکنی، لیس دار چیز میں نجاست داخل نہیں ہوتی بلغم بھی چونکہ چکنا ہوتا ہے تو اس میں بھی نجاست سرایت نہیں کرے گی اور وضو نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔ جو نجاست اس کے ساتھ لگی ہوتی ہے وہ قلیل مقدار میں ہے اور قی میں قلیل مقدار ناقض وضو نہیں ہے، لہذا البلغم کی قی بھی ناقض وضو نہیں ہوگی۔
لا تتخلله النجاسة أى لا يتداخله النجاسة ولا يدخل فى أجزائه وما يتصل به قليل والقليل في القيء غير ناقض لأنه لا يحتمل السيلان والسيلان في غير السبيلين أقيم مقام الخروج ولم يوجده.
[البناية: كتاب الطهارة، فصل فى نواقض الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۲۷۶، ط: دار الكتب العلمية]
لأنه بزاق حقيقة والبزاق طاهر لأن الرطوبة ترقى أعلى الحلق فتصير بزاقا وفي أسفله تغلظ فتصير بلغما فلم يخرج من المعدة ولئن خرج منها فهو لزج صقيل لا تتخلله النجاسة وما يتصل به منها قليل وهو في القيء عفو.
حاشية الطحطاوي على مراقي الفلاح: فصل: عشرة أشياء لا تنقض الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۹۴، ط: دار الكتب العلمية

The gist of the proof is that according to both parties (the Imams), if a person vomits phlegm, then his wudu does not break. The reason is that phlegm has smoothness, and the principle is that impurity does not enter into a smooth, sticky substance. Since phlegm is also smooth, impurity will not penetrate it and wudu will not break. The impurity that sticks to it is in a small amount, and a small amount in vomit is not a nullifier of wudu; therefore, vomiting of phlegm is also not a nullifier of wudu.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary on Al-Hidayah. It addresses whether vomiting phlegm nullifies wudu. The ruling is that it does not. The proof is twofold: first, phlegm is smooth and sticky, which prevents impurity from penetrating (a characteristic of thick substances). Second, whatever impurity accompanies the phlegm is a small amount, and small amounts of impurity in vomit are excused according to the Hanafi school. The text then cites two authoritative sources: from Al-Binayah stating that impurity does not penetrate phlegm, and from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi explaining that phlegm is essentially saliva that thickens, and even if it comes from the stomach, it is viscous and not penetrated by impurity; any attached impurity is minimal and forgiven in vomit. Thus, the ruling is established through rational reasoning (istihsan) based on the physical properties of phlegm and the principle of minimal impurity.

Key Terms
بلغم (balgham): Thick phlegm or mucus that may be expelled during vomiting, considered a smooth (chikna) substance.
قی (qay'): Vomiting, the expulsion of stomach contents.
ناقض وضو (naqid al-wudu): An action or occurrence that nullifies the state of wudu, requiring its renewal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is the primary reason why phlegm does not break wudu?
A) Because phlegm is not considered 'vomit' at all.
B) Because phlegm is smooth and impurity cannot penetrate it.
C) Because there is no impurity present in phlegm.
D) Because the Prophet ﷺ specifically exempted phlegm.

Key Takeaway
Vomiting phlegm does not break wudu because its smoothness prevents impurity penetration, and any attached impurity is minimal and excused.

The Stomach Is Not a Place for Blood: Internal Bleeding and Wudu
Establishing the ruling that blood from the stomach via a wound nullifies wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on blood from the stomach and explain the qiyas used by the two Shaykhs.

Core Idea
Blood from the stomach nullifies wudu because the stomach is not its normal place—it must be from a wound.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses an intra-madhhab difference: whether blood that emerges from the stomach (e.g., by vomiting) breaks wudu. The two Shaykhs analogize it to external wound bleeding, while Abu Hanifa reportedly held a different view. Let’s explore the reasoning and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
State the precise legal question: does blood that emerges from the stomach and flows by its own force nullify wudu?
2
Locate the Hanafi position
Identify the two Shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) and their ruling: such blood nullifies wudu even if the amount is small, because the stomach is not a natural place for blood.
The condition of flow (sayalan) is key.
3
Trace the dalil
The proof is qiyas (analogy). The stomach is not a place for blood, so the blood must originate from a wound. External wound blood nullifies wudu when it flows; therefore, this internal wound blood also nullifies wudu when it flows.
The illa is that the blood is from an unnatural place, indicating a wound.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa's reported position: he may require the blood to be from the two passages (front/back) or in a large amount to nullify wudu.
The exact details of Abu Hanifa's view are not elaborated in this text.
5
Determine the tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of the two Shaykhs, as confirmed in Al-Bada'i' and followed by the generality of later mashayikh.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa in the Hanafi school.

Source Text

ترجمہ: معدہ خون کی جگہ نہیں ہے۔ تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ نے اصول مذکورہ کو دلیل عقلی کے پیش نظر ذکر کر کے حضرات شیخین کا مسلک ثابت کیا ہے کہ اگر خون اپنی قوت کی وجہ سے بہہ جائے تو وہ ناقض وضو ہے۔ حاصل یہ ہے کہ حضرات شیخین کے نزدیک اگر خون اپنی قوت سے اپنی جگہ سے بہہ جائے اگر چہ وہ خون قلیل مقدار میں ہی کیوں نہ ہو تو اس سے وضو ٹوٹے گا، اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ معدہ خون کی جگہ نہیں ہے، جب معدہ محلِ دم نہیں تو لا محالہ یہی کہا جائے گا کہ یہ نکلنے والا خون پیٹ کے کسی زخم سے نکلا ہے، پس اس کو اس خون پر قیاس کیا جائے گا جو ظاہر زخم سے نکلے، چونکہ اس میں

لأن المعدة ليست بمحل للدم يعنى أنها ليست من مظان الدم ومواضعه فتكون من قرحة في الجوف فالمعتبر هناك السيلان فکذلک .هناک
[البناية: كتاب الطهارة، فصل فى نواقض الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۲۲، ط: دار الكتب العلمية]
وَعِنْدَهُمَا إِنْ سَالَ بِقُوَّةِ نَفْسِهِ نَقَضَ الْوُضُوءَ، وَإِنْ كَانَ قَلِيلًا، لأَنَّ الْمِعْدَةَ لَيْسَتْ بِمَحَلَّ الدَّمِ فَيَكُونُ مِنْ قُرُحَةٍ فِي الْجَوْفِ كَذَا فِى الْهِدَايَةِ وَاخْتَلَفَ التَّصْحِيحُ فَصَحَّحَ فِي الْبَدَائِعِ قَوْلَهُمَا قَالَ وَبِهِ أَخَذَ عَامَّةُ الْمَشَايِخِ.
[البحر الرائق: كتاب الطهارة، فصل فى نواقض الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۳۷، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي]
الهداية كتاب الطهارة، ج ا ص ۲۶، ط: رحمانيه
(11) ‘Verily the stomach is not a place for blood.’ Translation: The stomach is not a place for blood. Explanation: The author of Al-Hidayah mentioned this principle as a rational proof, and established the position of the two Shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) that if blood flows by its own force, then it nullifies wudu. The gist is that according to the two Shaykhs, if blood flows from its place by its own force, even if it is a small amount, then wudu breaks. The reason is that the stomach is not a place for blood. Since the stomach is not a place for blood, inevitably it will be said that this exiting blood came from a wound in the belly. So it will be analogized to blood that exits from an apparent wound. Because in that case, flow (sayalan) is necessary for nullification of wudu, and here flow is also found. Therefore, merely due to flow, the ruling of nullifying wudu will be applied; here the condition of multiplicity (?) is not required.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning blood emerging from the stomach. The author of Al-Hidayah brings forward the principle that the stomach is not a normal place for blood (i.e., blood is not supposed to be in the stomach). Therefore, if blood emerges from the stomach (e.g., through vomiting or passing), it must be from a wound or injury in the internal cavity. The two Shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that such blood, if it flows by its own force, breaks wudu regardless of quantity, because it is analogous to blood from an external wound which also breaks wudu when it flows. The School's relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that of the two Shaykhs, as confirmed in Al-Bada'i', and most later scholars (mashayikh) follow this. The text also clarifies that for blood to nullify wudu, flow (sayalan) is the operative factor, not the amount. This is a classic example of qiyas: analogizing internal bleeding to external wound bleeding based on the common cause of coming from a non-normal place.

Key Terms
معدہ (mi'da): Stomach; the organ where digestion occurs, not a natural place for blood.
قرحہ (qurha): Wound or ulcer in the internal cavity (jawf).
سیلان (sayalan): Flow; the movement of blood from its source by its own force.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the underlying principle that leads the two Shaykhs (Sahibayn) to rule that blood from the stomach nullifies wudu?
A) The stomach is a place for blood digestion, so any blood there is impure.
B) The stomach is not a natural place for blood, so the blood must come from a wound and is analogous to external wound bleeding.
C) Blood from any internal organ is purer than external blood.
D) The stomach produces blood naturally.

Key Takeaway
Blood from the stomach nullifies wudu because the stomach is not a natural place for blood; the relied-upon Hanafi view follows the two Shaykhs.

Customary Certainty: Sleeping on the Side Breaks Wudu
Applies the principle of customary certainty to rule that sleeping on the side nullifies wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle 'al-thabit 'aadatan ka al-muta'ayyan bihi' and apply it to determine when sleep nullifies wudu, using the illa of istirkha' al-mafasil.

Core Idea
Customary wind release during side sleeping is treated as certain, thus wudu breaks.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi evidentiary principle: what is established by custom is treated as certainty in law. The author of al-Hidayah applies this to sleeping on one's side, ruling that it nullifies wudu because relaxation of joints customarily leads to wind release. We will work through the reasoning, the principle's basis, and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is sleeping on one's side (inditija') or any position that relaxes the joints (istirkha' al-mafasil). The ruling is that this nullifies wudu (yantaqid al-wudu).
The ruling is not that sleep itself breaks wudu, but a specific posture.
2
Locate the dalil (principle)
The author cites the principle 'al-thabit 'aadatan ka al-muta'ayyan bihi': what is customarily established is like that which is certainly established. This is a Hanafi legal maxim used in evidentiary reasoning.
This principle is not from primary sources (Quran/Sunnah) but from usul al-fiqh based on custom (urf).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The cause (illa) is relaxation of joints during side sleeping, which customarily leads to the release of wind (khuruj al-rih). Since wind release definitely breaks wudu, and the custom reliably indicates its occurrence, we treat it as certain.
The Hanafi school uses customary occurrence as a ground for legal certainty when direct knowledge is absent.
4
Apply the analogy of entering the toilet
The text gives a parallel: if someone enters a toilet and later doubts his wudu, he is judged to have broken it because it is customary to relieve oneself there. Similarly, side sleeping triggers the customary release of wind.
This shows the principle is consistently applied across different contexts.
5
Note cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i, Maliki) may not accept customary occurrence as a basis for certainty; they often require actual awareness of wind release. Thus, the Hanafi position is more far-reaching.
This difference results in practical variations in daily wudu rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۲) إِنَّ الثَّابِتَ عَادَةٌ كَالْمُتَيَقَّنِ بِهِ.
ترجمہ: جو چیز عادتاً ثابت ہو وہ یقینی طور پر ثابت شدہ چیز کی طرح ہے۔
تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ نے اصول ذکر کر کے اس بات کی طرف اشارہ کیا ہے کہ کروٹ پر لیٹ کر سونا ناقض وضو ہے۔ جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ اگر کوئی شخص کروٹ کے بل یا تکیہ وغیرہ لگا کر سو گیا تو اس کا وضو ٹوٹ جائے گا، اس کی وجہ صاحب ہدایہ نے ذکر کی ہے کہ کروٹ پر سونا جوڑ بند کے ڈھیلے ہونے کا سبب ہے اور اس صورت میں عام طور پر کوئی نہ کوئی چیز نکل جاتی ہے، پس کروٹ پر سونا عادتا کسی چیز کے نکلنے سے خالی نہیں ہوتا، اور اصول ہے کہ جو چیز عادتاً ثابت ہو وہ یقین کا درجہ رکھتی ہے۔ یہ بات مسلم ہے کہ کروٹ پر لیٹنے سے استرخاء مفاصل کی وجہ سے خروج ریح و غیرہ ہوتی ہے اور خروج ریح سے یقینی طور پر وضو ٹوٹ جاتا ہے، چونکہ انضطجاع کی صورت میں بھی استرخاء مفاصل ہو جاتا ہے، لہذا اس صورت میں بھی وضو ٹوٹ جائے گا۔
لان الاضْطِجَاعَ سَبَبٌ لِاسْتِرُخَاءِ الْمَفَاصِلِ فَلَا يَخْلُو عَنْ خُرُوجِ رِيحٍ عَادَةً، وَالثَّابِتُ عَادَةً كَالْمُتَيَقَّنِ بِهِ، أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّ مَنْ دَخَلَ الْمُسْتَرَاحَ ثُمَّ شَكٍّ فِي وُضُوئِهِ فَإِنَّهُ يُحْكَمُ بِنَقْضِ وُضُوئِهِ، لِأَنَّ الْعَادَةَ جَرَتْ عِنْدَ الدُّخُولِ فِى الْخَلَاءِ بِالتَّبَرُّزِ ، بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا شَكٍّ بِدُونِ الدُّخُولِ.
[العناية: كتاب الطهارة، فصل فى نواقض الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۴۷، ط: دار الفکر]
(12) ‘Verily, what is established by custom is like what is established with certainty.’ Translation: A thing established by custom is like a thing established with certainty. Explanation: The author of Al-Hidayah mentioned this principle to indicate that sleeping on one’s side (kurrot) is a nullifier of wudu. The gist is that if a person sleeps on his side or leaning on a pillow etc., his wudu breaks. The reason given by the author is that sleeping on the side causes the joints to loosen, and in that state, something usually escapes. Thus sleeping on the side is customarily not free from something exiting. And the principle is that what is established by custom has the rank of certainty. It is accepted that lying on the side causes relaxation of joints, resulting in the release of wind etc., and the release of wind definitely breaks wudu. Since in the case of lying down (inditija') relaxation of joints also occurs, therefore in this case wudu will break.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents the principle of customary certainty and its application to the ruling on sleeping. The principle states that if something is habitually or customarily true (e.g., that wind exits when sleeping on one's side), it is treated as a certainty for legal rulings, even if one does not actually perceive the event. The author applies this to sleeping on the side (al-istitja') or leaning on a pillow, which causes relaxation of the joints (istirkha' al-mafasil). Since it is customary that wind escapes during such sleep, we assume it happened and therefore wudu is nullified. The text also gives an analogous example: if a person enters a toilet and then doubts his wudu, he is judged to have broken it because it is customary to relieve oneself when entering a toilet. This is contrasted with doubting without having entered a toilet. Thus, the principle is a rule of evidence in the Hanafi school: customary occurrence gives legal certainty.

Key Terms
انضطجاع (inditija'): Lying on one's side or sleeping in a reclining position that relaxes the joints.
استرخاء مفاصل (istirkha' al-mafasil): Relaxation of the joints, which occurs during sleep and may lead to involuntary release of wind.
ثابت بالعادة (thabit bil-'aada): Established by custom; a legal presumption based on habitual occurrence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does sleeping on one's side break wudu according to the principle discussed?
A) Because sleeping always breaks wudu regardless of position.
B) Because sleeping on the side causes relaxation of joints, and it is customary that wind escapes; this is treated as certain.
C) Because the person is unconscious and cannot maintain wudu.
D) Because it is a form of impurity.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, custom (urf) can establish legal certainty: sleeping on the side nullifies wudu because wind release is customary, even if not directly perceived.

The Maxim of Habitual Certainty and Its Application to Wudu Nullification
Explains the maxim and applies it to two nullification cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that sleeping on the side nullifies wudu and explain how the maxim 'al-thabit 'adatan ka al-mutayaqqan bihi' serves as its proof. Additionally, you will be able to distinguish the intra-Hanafi positions on whether blood from the stomach nullifies wudu and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A habitually certain event legally equals actual certainty — sleeping on the side breaks wudu because wind release is habitual.

This lesson introduces a foundational Hanafi legal maxim that elevates habitual occurrence to the status of certainty. The passage from Al-Hidayah applies this maxim to two cases: sleeping on the side and blood emerging from the stomach. Understanding this maxim is crucial for correctly determining wudu nullification in situations where actual knowledge is absent but habit provides a reliable indicator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim
Locate the Arabic statement of the maxim in the passage: 'إِنَّ الثَّابِتَ عَادَةٌ كَالْمُتَيَقَّنِ بِهِ'. Understand its meaning: what is habitually established is treated like what is certain.
The maxim is a rational principle derived from inductive observation of human behavior.
2
Apply the maxim to sleeping on the side
The author explains that lying on the side causes relaxation of joints (istirkha' al-mafasil), which habitually leads to the release of wind. Therefore, wudu is nullified even without sensing the wind.
The habit is so strong that it suffices to rule nullification.
3
State the ruling for sleeping on the side
Conclude that sleeping on the side (or leaning on a pillow) nullifies wudu. This is the mufta bihi position.
This is a clear ruling with no disagreement among Hanafis.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on blood from the stomach
The passage notes that Abu Hanifa requires actual flow (sayalan) for blood to nullify wudu, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that any blood that flows by itself nullifies, even a small amount, because the stomach is not a natural blood site.
The dispute hinges on whether the stomach is akin to a wound.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Later scholars, as cited from Al-Bada'i', prefer the view of the two imams. Thus, in practice, any blood that exits by itself from the stomach nullifies wudu.
Tarjih authority: Al-Kasani in Al-Bada'i'.

نقض وضو کے لئے سیلان ضروری ہے اور یہاں پر بھی سیلان پایا گیا ہے ، اس لئے محض سیلان سے نقض وضو کا حکم لگایا جائے گا، اس میں ملا لنظم کی شرط نہیں ہے۔
(۱۲) إِنَّ الثَّابِتَ عَادَةٌ كَالْمُتَيَقَّنِ بِهِ.
ترجمہ: جو چیز عادتاً ثابت ہوہ یقینی طور پر ثابت شدہ چیز کی طرح ہے۔
تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ نے اصول ذکر کر کے اس بات کی طرف اشارہ کیا ہے کہ کروٹ پر لیٹ کر سونا ناقض وضو ہے۔ جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ اگر کوئی شخص کروٹ کے بل یا تکیہ وغیر ہ لگا کر سو گیا تو اس کا وضو ٹوٹ جائے گا، اس کی وجہ صاحب ہدایہ نے ذکر کی ہے کہ کروٹ پر سونا جوڑ بند کے ڈھیلے ہونے کا سبب ہے اور اس صورت میں عام طور پر کوئی نہ کوئی چیز نکل جاتی ہے، پس کروٹ پر سونا عادتا کسی چیز کے نکلنے سے خالی نہیں ہوتا ، اور اصول ہے کہ جو چیز عادتاً ثابت ہو وہ یقین کا درجہ رکھتی ہے۔ یہ بات مسلم ہے کہ کروٹ پر لیٹنے سے استرخاء مفاصل کی وجہ سے خروج ریح و غیرہ ہوتی ہے اور خروج ریح سے یقینی طور پر وضو ٹوٹ جاتا ہے، چونکہ انضطجاع کی صورت میں بھی استرخاء مفاصل ہو جاتا ہے ، لہذا اس صورت میں بھی وضو ٹوٹ جائے گا۔
(لأن المعدة ليست بمحل للدم يعنى أنها ليست من مظان الدم ومواضعه فتكون من قرحة في الجوف فالمعتبر هناك السيلان فکذلک .هناک
البناية: كتاب الطهارة، فصل فى نواقض الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۲۲، ط: دار الكتب العلمية وَعِنْدَهُمَا إِنْ سَالَ بِقُوَّةِ نَفْسِهِ نَقَضَ الْوُضُوءَ، وَإِنْ كَانَ قَلِيلًا، لأَنَّ الْمَعِدَةَ لَيْسَتْ بِمَحَلَّ الدَّمِ فَيَكُونُ مِنْ قُرُحَةٍ فِي الْجَوْفِ كَذَا فِى الْهِدَايَةِ وَاخْتَلَفَ التَّصْحِيحُ فَصَحَّحَ فِي الْبَدَائِعِ قَوْلَهُمَا قَالَ وَبِهِ أَخَذَ عَامَّةُ الْمَشَايِخِ. البحر الرائق: كتاب الطهارة، فصل فى نواقض الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۳۷، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي الهداية كتاب الطهارة ، ج ا ص ۲۶، ط: رحمانيه لان الاضْطِجَاعَ سَبَبٌ لِاسْتِرُخَاءِ الْمَفَاصِلِ فَلَا يَخْلُو عَنْ خُرُوجِ رِيحٍ عَادَةً، وَالثَّابِتُ عَادَةً كَالْمُتَيَقَّنِ بِهِ، أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّ مَنْ دَخَلَ الْمُسْتَرَاحَ ثُمَّ شَكٍّ فِي وُضُوئِهِ فَإِنَّهُ يُحْكَمُ بِنَقْضِ وُضُوئِهِ، لِأَنَّ الْعَادَةَ جَرَتْ عِنْدَ الدُّخُولِ فِى الْخَلَاءِ بِالتَّبَرُّزِ ، بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا شَكٍّ بِدُونِ الدُّخُولِ.
[العناية: كتاب الطهارة، فصل فى نواقض الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۴۷، ط: دار الفکر]
For wudu nullification, flow (sayalan) is necessary, and here flow is found, so the ruling of wudu nullification will be given based on mere flow; the condition of being mixed with something is not present. (12) Verily what is habitually established is like what is certain. Translation: A thing that is habitually established is like a thing that is certainly established. Explanation: The author of Al-Hidayah indicated by mentioning this principle that sleeping on the side is a nullifier of wudu. The summary is: If a person sleeps on his side or leaning on a pillow, his wudu will break. The reason mentioned by the author of Al-Hidayah is that sleeping on the side causes relaxation of joints, and in that situation something usually exits. So sleeping on the side is not free from something exiting habitually, and the principle is that what is habitually established holds the rank of certainty. It is accepted that lying on the side causes, due to relaxation of joints, the exit of wind and other things, and the exit of wind certainly breaks wudu. Since during sleeping on the side relaxation of joints occurs, therefore in that case wudu will break. (Because the stomach is not a place for blood, meaning it is not a source or location of blood, so it would be from a wound in the belly; so there the criterion is flow, and likewise here. Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Taharah, Fasl fi Nawaqid al-Wudu, vol. 1, p. 22, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah. And according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if it flows by itself, it breaks wudu, even if it is little, because the stomach is not a place for blood, so it would be from a wound in the belly. Thus it is in Al-Hidayah. There is a difference in verification; in Al-Bada'i' the view of the two is corrected, and it is said that most of the mashayikh adopted it. Al-Bahr al-Raiq: Kitab al-Taharah, Fasl fi Nawaqid al-Wudu, vol. 1, p. 37, Dar al-Kitab al-Islami. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Taharah, vol. 1, p. 26, Rahmaniyyah. Because lying down is a cause for relaxation of joints, so it is not free from the exit of wind habitually, and what is habitually established is like what is certain. Do you not see that if someone enters the toilet and then doubts his wudu, it is ruled that his wudu is nullified, because the habit when entering the privy is to relieve oneself, contrary to if he doubts without entering. [Al-'Inayah: Kitab al-Taharah, Fasl fi Nawaqid al-Wudu, vol. 1, p. 47, Dar al-Fikr]
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi legal maxim 'Al-Thabit 'Adatan Ka al-Mutayaqqan Bihi' (What is habitually established is like what is certain). The author applies it to justify that sleeping on one's side (or leaning on a pillow) breaks wudu, because it habitually causes the release of wind due to relaxation of joints. Since habit suffices as certainty, one does not need to actually sense the wind for wudu to be nullified. The passage also includes a citation from Al-Hidayah regarding a related ruling: when blood emerges from the stomach (not a normal site for blood), the two Imams (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that even a small amount of blood that flows by itself nullifies wudu, as it is treated as coming from a wound. Abu Hanifa restricts this to cases of flowing blood. The preferred view (mufta bihi) according to Al-Bada'i' is that of the two Imams. The principle is further illustrated by the example of someone entering the toilet: doubt about wudu after entering is resolved by ruling that wudu is nullified due to the habit of relieving oneself.

Key Terms
سیلان (sayalan): Flowing of blood such that it moves from its point of exit; a condition for wudu nullification according to Abu Hanifa.
استرخاء مفاصل (istirkha' al-mafasil): Relaxation of joints or muscles, which can cause involuntary release of wind.
عادت (adah): Habit; what normally and consistently occurs, legally treated as certain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'al-thabit 'adatan ka al-mutayaqqan bihi', why does sleeping on the side break wudu?
A) Because it is a direct cause of impurity.
B) Because it habitually causes wind release due to relaxation of joints.
C) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) explicitly said so.
D) Because it is a form of lying down.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-thabit 'adatan ka al-mutayaqqan bihi' allows the law to treat habitual occurrences as certain for the purpose of wudu nullification, as illustrated by sleeping on the side and blood from the stomach.

Al-Haraju Madfu'un Shar'an: Hardship Removal in Ghusl and Hajj
Explains the maxim and applies it to ghusl and Hajj.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim of hardship removal, apply it to the cases of braided hair in ghusl and frequent entry into Mecca without ihram, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i position on braided hair.

Core Idea
Hardship lifts obligation: a woman need not undo braids if water reaches roots; a resident may enter Mecca without ihram.

Sharia is built on ease, not hardship. The Hanafi school enshrines this principle in the maxim 'al-haraju madfu'un shar'an' – hardship is removed by law. This lesson unpacks the maxim through two practical rulings: the concession for a woman with braided hair during ghusl, and the concession for a resident of Mecca who enters the city frequently without ihram. Both demonstrate how the presence of hardship (haraj) cancels a normal obligation.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its basis
Begin by stating the Arabic maxim 'al-haraju madfu'un shar'an' and understand that it derives from Quranic verses (e.g., 22:78) and Sunnah that emphasize ease. This is a general principle, not a specific text.
The maxim is a qat'i thubut but zanni dalala – it's certain as a principle but its application requires ijtihad.
2
Identify the legal operation
The maxim exempts an obligatory act (wajib) when performing it would cause significant hardship. Determine what qualifies as haraj: here, undoing and wetting each strand of braided hair, or donning ihram for every daily trip into Mecca.
Hardship must be unusual; normal difficulty does not lift obligation.
3
Apply to the ghusl case
A woman with braided hair need not undo the braids nor wet every hair strand as long as water reaches the roots. The obligation to wash the hair remains, but the method is relaxed due to haraj. Contrast with a beard or loose hair: no hardship, so the full obligation stands.
The ruling is mufta bihi in the Hanafi school, though the Shafi'is require wetting every strand.
4
Apply to the Hajj case
A person whose home lies between the miqat and Mecca may enter Mecca without ihram for daily needs because requiring ihram each time would cause clear hardship. This is another direct application of the maxim.
This applies only when the person repeatedly enters Mecca; a single visit from outside the miqat still requires ihram.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school generally requires water to reach every hair strand in ghusl, regardless of braiding. The Hanafi concession is not universally accepted. Understand that the difference stems from how each school defines and weighs hardship.
The maxim itself is agreed upon, but its application differs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۳) إِنَّ الْحَرَجَ مَدْفُوعٌ شَرُعًا.
ترجمہ: یقیناً شریعت نے حرج کو دور کر رکھا ہے۔
تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ نے مذکورہ اصول سے اس بات کو ثابت کیا ہے کہ جس چیز میں شرعاً کوئی حرج ہو تو اسے بجالانا واجب نہیں ہے اور جس میں شرعاً کوئی حرج وغیرہ نہ ہو تو اُسے بجالانا ضروری ہوگا۔
صاحب ہدایہ نے اسے ایک مثال سے واضح فرمایا ہے کہ عورت کے لئے غسل کے وقت گوندھے ہوئے بالوں کا کھولنا ضروری نہیں ہے بشرطیکہ پانی بالوں کی جڑوں تک پہنچ جاتا ہو، تو اسی طرح اس کے لئے اپنے بٹے ہوئے گیسوؤں کو تر کرنا بھی ضروری نہیں ہے ، اس لئے کہ ان بے ہوئے بالوں کو تر کرنے میں عورت کے حق میں حرج ہے اور شریعت نے حرج کو دور کر رکھا ہے، جیسا کہ اصول مذکورہ میں موجود ہے کہ جس چیز میں شرعاً حرج ہو تو اُسے بجالانا ضروری نہیں ہے، اور یہاں پر بھی چونکہ عورت کے لئے اپنے بٹے ہوئے بالوں کو تر کرنے میں حرج ہے اس لئے ان بالوں کو تر کرنا ضروری نہیں ہے، بخلاف داڑھی کے کہ اس کے اندر پانی پہنچانے میں کوئی حرج اور مشقت نہیں ہے، لہذا داڑھی کے اندر پانی کا پہنچانا واجب اور ضروری ہوگا۔ اسی طرح اگر عورت کے بال کھلے ہوں تو ان کے درمیان بھی پانی پہنچانا واجب ہے، کیونکہ اب اس میں کوئی حرج اور مشقت نہیں ہے۔
الهداية: كتاب الطهارات، فصل فى الغسل، ج ۱ ص ۳۱، ط: رحمانيه لأَنَّ مَوَاضِعَ الضَّرُورَةِ مُسْتَشَاةٌ كَدَاخِلِ الْعَيْنَيْنِ، وَأَمَّا بَلُّهَا فَكَذَلِكَ فِي الصَّحِيحِ لِمَا فِيهِ مِنْ الْحَرَج.
العناية: كتاب الطهارة، فصل في الغسل ج ۱ ص ۵۹، ط: دار الفکر
ومن كان داخل الميقات أى ومن كان وطنه بين الميقات ومكة له أن يدخل مكة بغير إحرام لحاجته أى لأجل حاجته لأنه يكثر دخوله مكة، وفى إيجاب الإحرام في كل مرة حرج بين أى ظاهر، والحرج مدفوع شرعاً. [البناية: كتاب الحج، فصل المواقيت المكانية للحج، ج ۴ ص ۱۶۳، ط: دار الكتب العلمية]
(13) Verily hardship is repelled in Sharia. Translation: Indeed, Sharia has removed hardship. Explanation: The author of Al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, proves that any matter in which there is hardship in Sharia does not need to be performed, and any matter in which there is no hardship necessarily does need to be performed. The author of Al-Hidayah clarified this with an example: A woman does not need to undo her braided hair during ghusl provided that water reaches the roots of the hair. Similarly, she does not need to wet her plaited locks because wetting them would involve hardship. Since there is hardship in wetting plaited hair, it is not necessary, contrary to the beard: there is no hardship in making water reach it, so it is obligatory to make water reach inside the beard. Similarly, if a woman's hair is loose, it is obligatory to make water reach between the strands because there is no hardship. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Taharat, Fasl fi al-Ghusl, vol. 1, p. 31, Rahmaniyyah: 'Because the places of necessity are exempted, like the inside of the eyes; and as for wetting them, it is similar in the correct view due to the hardship involved.' Al-'Inayah: Kitab al-Taharah, Fasl fi al-Ghusl, vol. 1, p. 59, Dar al-Fikr. 'And one who is inside the miqat, i.e., one whose homeland is between the miqat and Mecca, may enter Mecca without ihram for his need, because he enters Mecca frequently, and requiring ihram each time would be clear hardship, and hardship is repelled by Sharia.' [Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Hajj, Fasl al-Mawaqit al-Makaniyyah li al-Hajj, vol. 4, p. 163, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah]
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi legal maxim 'Al-Haraju Madfu'un Shar'an' (Hardship is removed by Sharia). The principle is that any act involving significant hardship is not obligatory. The author provides two applications: (1) In ghusl, a woman with braided hair does not need to undo it or wet each strand individually if water can reach the roots through the braids. This is because requiring her to do so would cause hardship. However, for a man's beard or a woman's loose hair, where there is no hardship, water must reach every part. (2) In Hajj, a person whose home is located between the miqat and Mecca may enter Mecca without ihram for his daily needs, as requiring ihram each time would be a clear hardship. The citations from Al-Hidayah and Al-Binayah reinforce these rulings by invoking the same maxim.

Key Terms
حرج (haraj): Hardship or constraint that makes an obligation difficult to perform without undue trouble.
مشقت (mashaqqah): Exertion or difficulty; synonymous with haraj in this context.
مستشاة (mustashah): Places of necessity or concession; areas where strict requirements are relaxed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'al-haraju madfu'un shar'an', why does a woman with braided hair not need to undo her hair during ghusl?
A) Because braided hair is not required to be washed.
B) Because undoing and wetting each strand would involve hardship.
C) Because the Quran specifically exempts braided hair.
D) Because women are not obligated to wash their hair at all.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-haraju madfu'un shar'an' exempts an obligatory act when its performance entails significant hardship, as exemplified by the ghusl concession for braided hair and the Hajj concession for frequent entry into Mecca.
